The Linguistic Argument: How 'مع' Can Indicate Delay in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Explaining the linguistic argument for 'مع' indicating delay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument using the Quranic verse 'إن مع العسر يسرا' to show that 'مع' can indicate delay and apply it to resolve objections.

Core Idea
The Quranic usage of 'مع' after hardship proves it can mean delay, supporting the Hanafi interpretation in conditional divorce.

This lesson focuses on the linguistic debate within the passage. An objector claims that 'مع' always implies simultaneity. The Hanafi response uses a Quranic verse to show that 'مع' can indicate temporal delay. We will analyze this argument, the counterargument, and the final ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent says the word 'مع' in the divorce phrase is for conjunction (simultaneity), so emancipation and divorce should occur together.
The passage says: 'فإن قيل: كلمة مع للقران'.
2
Present the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response uses the Quranic verse 'إن مع العسر يسرا' to show that 'مع' can indicate delay, as ease comes after hardship.
The verse is from Surah al-Sharh (94:6).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'مع' in the verse is clearly understood to mean after, not simultaneous. This provides linguistic evidence that 'مع' can be used for delay.
4
Address the counterargument
The opponent concedes that 'مع' can mean delay, but argues it is not the default meaning and may not apply here. The Hanafi rebuttal invokes context (qarina): the conditional nature of the statement forces the interpretation of delay.
The commentator says: 'إن قرينة المقام اقتضت جعله بمعنى التأخير'.
5
Distinguish the counterexample
The case from al-Jami' where a husband says 'You are divorced with your marriage' is different. There, divorce and marriage are contradictory, so 'مع' cannot be interpreted as a condition. In our case, emancipation and divorce can be sequential, so the conditional interpretation is valid.
This distinction reinforces the Hanafi position.

[٤] فإن قيل : كلمة : مع ؛ للقران.
قلنا : جاءت للتأخير نحو قوله تعالى : إِنَّ مَعَ العِرَ) (۱) .
(۱) قوله : أمة غيره ؛ هذا وإن لم يكن مذكوراً في المتن إلا أنه مفهوم من قوله : مع عتق سيدك»، فإنّ مثل هذا لا يستقيم في الحرّة. [٢] قوله : جعل شرطاً ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الشرط عبارة عما يكون معدوماً على خطر الوجود، ويكون الحكم متعلّقاً به وهذا الأمر موجود هاهنا، فيكون إعتاق المولى شرطاً للطلاق، وإن لم يصرح به، فإنّ العبرة للمعاني لا للألفاظ. فإن قلت: لم يذكر في المسألة إعتاق المولى. قلت : العتق يجيء بمعنى الإعتاق. فإن قلت : أين التطليق في الكلام حتى يجعل معلقاً بالإعتاق. قلت : تعليق الحكم يقتضي تعليق سببه، فإذا علّق الطلاق بأمر اقتضى تعليق سببه وهو التطليق به. [٣] قوله : مقدماً ؛ لكون الشرط مقدماً على الجزاء. [٤] قوله : فإن قيل ... الخ ؛ إيراد على ما مرَّ من كون العتق مقدّماً على الطلاق ، وحاصله أنّ كلمة «مع» موضوعة للقرآن ؛ أي الاجتماع والمعيّة من دونِ دلالة على تقدم ما قبله أو بعده ، فقوله : أنت طالق ثنتين مع عتق سيدك لك» يدلُّ على مقارنة الطلاق والعتق ، فلا يصح جعلُ العتق مقدّماً. [٥]قوله : قلنا ... الخ ؛ حاصل الجواب : أنّ وضع «مع» وإن كان للمعية لكنها تستعمل في تأخير ما بعدها عما قبلها أيضاً، كما في قوله : فَإِنَّ مَعَ العُسرِ يُسرًا (۱) من سورة الشرح، الآية (٦). فإنّ مع هاهنا مستعملة للتأخير؛ لظهور أن اليسر إنما يكون بعد العسر لا معه. ويرد هاهنا أنا سلّمنا مجيء «مع» للتأخير، لكنه ليس كلياً ولا وضعاً، فيجوز أن لا يكون في القول المذكور مستعملاً فيه. وجوابه : إن قرينة المقام اقتضت هاهنا جعله بمعنى التأخير؛ لوجود موجبه وهو تحقق المعنى الشرطي كما مرّ، وقال العيني في «البناية»: إنّ كلمة «مع» قد تذكر للاقتران في زمان الوجود، وقد تذكر للاقتران في أصل الوجود كما في قوله : وَأَسْلَمْتُ مَعَ سُلَيْمَانَ لِلَّهِ رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ ) (٢) ، وكما في قوله : ( إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا ( ٢ فلو كان المراد هو الأول تحرم حرمة غليظة، ولو كان المراد هو الثاني لم تحرم والحرمة الغليظة لم تكن ثابتة فلا تثبت بالشك والاحتمال. فإن قيل : ينتقض هذا بما ذكر في «الجامع» بقوله لامرأته : أنت طالق مع نكاحك، حيث لم يصح ولم يصر بمعنى الشرط، فينبغي أن يكون كذلك هاهنا. أجيب : بأن العدول عن حقيقة معنى القران باعتبار أنه ملك التطليق تنجيزاً وتعليقاً، فكان من ضرورة كلامه أن يحمل على معنى الشرط. أما هاهنا لم يملك الطلاق، والطلاق مع النكاح متنافيان، فلا يلزم العدول عن معنى القرآن، فيلغو ضرورة
And he returns in 'You are divorced twice with the emancipation of your master for you, if he emancipates': A man married a slave woman and said to her: 'You are divorced twice with the emancipation of your master of you.' Thus the emancipation is advanced before the occurrence of the divorce, so the divorce occurs while she is free, so her divorce becomes three, and the husband retains the right of return. Conditional Divorce and Prior Emancipation: The Hanafi Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce after emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce when the condition is the same as a prior emancipation condition and explain why Imam Muhammad differs.

Core Idea
When a husband conditions divorce on the same future event as a master's emancipation, the divorce does not occur.

This lesson examines a nuanced case where a slave woman's emancipation and divorce are both conditioned on the same future event. The Hanafi school, contrary to Imam Muhammad, rules that the divorce does not take effect. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling and the intra-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A master conditions emancipation of his married slave woman upon the arrival of tomorrow. Later, the husband says to her: 'You are divorced upon the coming of tomorrow.'
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and the relied-upon view) is that the divorce does not occur when tomorrow arrives.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the divorce does occur. The passage explicitly states 'contrary to Muhammad'.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Understand the legal basis
The condition for divorce (mu'allaq 'alayh) is the same as the condition for emancipation. Unity of the conditioned-upon event makes the second condition ineffective — the divorce is deemed not to occur.
The word 'with' (مع) in earlier context implies delay, but here the focus is on the single condition.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The relied-upon ruling for fatwa is that the divorce does not occur. This is the view of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
Imam Muhammad's view is recognised but not adopted as mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند مجيء غد، بعد تعليق عتقها وتطليقها بمجيئه،لا، خلافاً لمحمد (وعند مجيء غد"، بعد تعليق عتقها" إنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا) . فإنّ مع هاهنا مستعملة للتأخير؛ لظهور أن اليسر إنما يكون بعد العسر لا معه. [١]قوله : وعند مجيء غد؛ هذا على سبيل التمثيل، وإلا فالمدار اتحاد المعلق عليه. [٢] قوله : بعد تعليق عتقها؛ هذا من المولى بأن عَلقَ عتق أمته المتزوجة بمجيء الغد، وقال: إذا جاءَ الغد فأنت حرّة.
And upon the coming of tomorrow, after conditioning her emancipation and her divorce upon its coming, no, contrary to Muhammad. (And upon the coming of tomorrow, after conditioning her emancipation...) Indeed, with hardship is ease. For 'with' here is used for delay, because it is apparent that ease occurs after hardship, not with it. [1] His saying: 'Upon the coming of tomorrow' – this is by way of example, otherwise the criterion is the unity of the conditioned-upon thing. [2] His saying: 'after conditioning her emancipation' – this is from the master, that he conditioned the emancipation of his married slave woman upon the coming of tomorrow, and said: 'When tomorrow comes, you are free.'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where the master had previously conditioned the emancipation of his married slave woman on the arrival of the next day. Then the husband says to her: 'You are divorced upon the coming of tomorrow.' The ruling is that the divorce does not occur (لا) when tomorrow comes, contrary to Imam Muhammad's view. The commentary explains that the word 'with' in the earlier context is used for delay, but here the focus is on the fact that the divorce is conditioned on the same event as the emancipation. The master's condition of emancipation upon the arrival of tomorrow means that when tomorrow comes, she becomes free. However, the husband's divorce condition is also on the same event. The Hanafi ruling (opposite to Muhammad) is that the divorce does not occur because the condition for divorce is the same as the condition for emancipation, and the divorce cannot be effective after emancipation since she is no longer his wife? Or perhaps because the divorce is conditional on the same event as a prior condition, the second condition is invalid. The text is brief but indicates an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Key Terms
معلق عليه (mu'allaq 'alayh): The condition on which a contingent statement (like divorce or emancipation) is based.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Where a conditional divorce is tied to the same future event as a prior conditional emancipation, the divorce is ineffective according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Simultaneous Conditional Emancipation and Divorce: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Raj'a
Commentary on simultaneous conditional acts and their legal consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position on simultaneous conditional emancipation and divorce from the opposing narration of Imam Muhammad, and explain the practical consequence on the husband's right of return.

Core Idea
When emancipation and divorce share the same condition, both occur together; the husband loses raj'a because the divorce happens while the wife is still a slave.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute at the intersection of conditional emancipation (by the master) and conditional divorce (by the husband) when both hinge on the same future event—here, the arrival of tomorrow. The central question is whether the husband retains the right of return (raj'a) after two divorces occur simultaneously with her emancipation. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the position of Abu Hanifa and contrasts it with a divergent narration attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the conditions
Note that the master conditions emancipation on 'tomorrow' and the husband conditions a double divorce on the same 'tomorrow'. When tomorrow arrives, both conditions are satisfied.
The condition is a future time; neither party mentions the other's act.
2
Determine the timing of the acts
According to the passage, both emancipation and divorce occur simultaneously upon the arrival of tomorrow. There is no priority—neither act is tied to the other.
This distinguishes it from the case where divorce is conditioned on the master's emancipation.
3
Apply the ruling on slave-woman divorces
A slave-woman becomes irrevocably separated (bainuna mughallaza) after two divorces. Since she is still a slave at the moment of divorce, the two divorces make her permanently forbidden to the husband without tahlil.
For a free woman, three divorces cause irrevocable separation.
4
Assess the right of return (raj'a)
Because the divorce occurred while she was a slave, the husband cannot take her back during her iddah. The right of return exists only after one revocable divorce (for a slave-woman) or two (for a free woman).
Here, two divorces have been pronounced, so no raj'a remains.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad is reported to disagree on the narration of Abu Hafs al-Kabir, but on the narration of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani there is no disagreement. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa: no raj'a.
The disagreement is considered weak (nawadir), not from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند مجيء غد، بعد تعليق عتقها وتطليقها بمجيئه، لا، خلافاً لمحمد (وعند مجيء غد، بعد تعليق عتقها) إنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا (١) ، فإنّ مع هاهنا مستعملة للتأخير؛ لظهور أن اليسر إنما يكون بعد العسر لا معه. ويرد هاهنا أنا سلّمنا مجيء «مع» للتأخير، لكنه ليس كلياً ولا وضعاً، فيجوز أن لا يكون في القول المذكور مستعملاً فيه. وجوابه : إن قرينة المقام اقتضت هاهنا جعله بمعنى التأخير؛ لوجود موجبه وهو تحقق المعنى الشرطي كما مرّ، وقال العيني في «البناية»: إنّ كلمة «مع» قد تذكر للاقتران في زمان الوجود، وقد تذكر للاقتران في أصل الوجود كما في قوله : وَأَسْلَمْتُ مَعَ آمَنَ لِلَّهِ رَبِّ الْعَالَمِينَ (٢) ، وكما في قوله : ( إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا ( ٢ فلو كان المراد هو الأول تحرم حرمة غليظة، ولو كان المراد هو الثاني لم تحرم والحرمة الغليظة لم تكن ثابتة فلا تثبت بالشك والاحتمال. فإن قيل : ينتقض هذا بما ذكر في «الجامع» بقوله لامرأته : أنت طالق مع نكاحك، حيث لم يصح ولم يصر بمعنى الشرط، فينبغي أن يكون كذلك هاهنا. أجيب : بأن العدول عن حقيقة معنى القران باعتبار أنه ملك التطليق تنجيزاً وتعليقاً، فكان من ضرورة كلامه أن يحمل على معنى الشرط. أما هاهنا لم يملك الطلاق، والطلاق مع النكاح متنافيان، فلا يلزم العدول عن معنى القرآن، فيلغو ضرورةً (٤). [١] قوله : وعند مجيء غد؛ هذا على سبيل التمثيل، وإلا فالمدار اتحاد المعلق عليه. [٢] قوله : بعد تعليق عتقها؛ هذا من المولى بأن علق عتق أمته المتزوجة بمجيء الغد، وقال: إذا جاء الغد فأنت حرة. وتطليقها بمجيئه لا، خلافاً لمحمد له، يعني لو قال المولى : إذا جاء الغد فأنت حرة، وقال الزوج : إذا جاء الغد فأنت طالق ثنتين، فجاء الغد، وقع العتق والطلاق، ولا يملك الزوج الرجعة؛ لأن وقوع العتق مقارن لوقوع الطلاق، فيقع الطلاق وهي أمة بخلاف المسألة الأولى. [١] قوله : وتطليقها؛ هذا من الزوج بأن علق طلاق امرأته الأمة بمجيء الغد سواء كان هذا التعليق قبل التعليق للولي أو بعده أو معه. [٢] قوله : لا؛ أي لا يراجع، فإنها تبين في هذه الصورة بينونة مغلظة لا يبقى معها ملك طلاق له عليها. [٣] قوله : خلافاً لمحمد؛ هذا على رواية أبي حفص الكبير، وأما على رواية أبي سليمان الجوزجاني فلا خلاف في المسألة. [٤] قوله : وقع؛ لوجود شرط العتق والطلاق، وهو مجيء الغد، وبالجملة : إذا توحد ما علق عليه المولى العتق، وما علق عليه الزوج الطلاق يقعان عند وجود الشرط معاً. [٥] قوله : ولا يملك؛ أي لا تجوز له الرجعة في العدة، وذلك لأن الرجعة إنما تجوز بعد اثنين في الحرة، وأما الأمة فلا رجعة فيها إلا بعد واحد، والاثنان تصير به بائنة بينونة مغلظة لا تحل له حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره، كالحرة بعد ثلاث، وهاهنا لما وقع العتق والطلاق معاً لم يبق ملك الرجعة، فإنه إنما يمكن إذا وقع الطلاق حالة حريتها. [٦] قوله : بخلاف المسألة الأولى؛ وهي ما إذا قال : أنت طالق ثنتين مع عتق مولاك، فأعتق المولى حيث يقع الاثنان ويملك الرجعة؛ لأن وقوع الطلاق هناك مرتب على العتق، فجعل العتق مقدماً والطلاق مؤخراً، فيكون الطلاق حالة حريتها. وأما هاهنا فليس كذلك، فإن الزوج لم يذكر في تعليقه عتق المولى، لا على سبيل الشرط، ولا على سبيل المعية، بل علقه بمجيء الغد، مع قطع النظر عن عتق المولى وعدمه، فلا يجعل العتق هاهنا شرطاً لوقوعه، حتى يعتبر تقدمه، والحال أن المولى علق عتقها أيضاً بذلك الشرط الذي علق به الزوج، فيقعان جميعاً عند وجود الشرط من غير لحاظ التقدم والتأخر.
And at the arrival of tomorrow, after conditioning her emancipation and divorcing her upon its arrival, no, contrary to Muhammad. ('And at the arrival of tomorrow', after conditioning her emancipation): Indeed, with hardship comes ease (1), for 'ma'a' here is used for postponement; because it is evident that ease only occurs after hardship, not with it. It is objected here: We concede that 'ma'a' comes for postponement, but it is not universal nor the primary meaning, so it is possible that in the mentioned statement it is not used for that. The answer: The context of the situation necessitates here making it to mean postponement; due to the existence of its cause, which is the realization of the conditional meaning as previously mentioned. And al-'Ayni said in al-Binayah: The word 'ma'a' may be mentioned for conjunction in the time of existence, and may be mentioned for conjunction in the origin of existence, as in His statement: 'And I submit with those who submit to the Lord of the Worlds' (2), and as in His statement: 'Indeed, with hardship comes ease.' (2) If the first meaning were intended, then a severe prohibition would occur; if the second meaning were intended, then no severe prohibition would occur, and the severe prohibition is not established, so it is not proven by doubt and probability. If it is said: This is contradicted by what is mentioned in al-Jami' concerning his statement to his wife: 'You are divorced with (ma'a) your marriage', as it was not valid and did not become conditional, so it should be the same here. The answer: The departure from the literal meaning of conjunction is because he had the power of divorce both in immediate and conditional terms, so his statement must be interpreted as conditional. But here he does not have the power of divorce, and divorce and marriage are contradictory, so the departure from the meaning of conjunction is not necessary, and it becomes void necessarily (4). [1] His statement: 'And at the arrival of tomorrow' — this is by way of example; otherwise the criterion is the unity of the conditioned-upon. [2] His statement: 'after conditioning her emancipation' — this is from the master, who conditioned the emancipation of his married slave-woman upon the arrival of tomorrow, saying: 'If tomorrow comes, you are free.' And divorcing her upon its arrival, no, contrary to Muhammad — meaning if the master said: 'If tomorrow comes, you are free', and the husband said: 'If tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice', and tomorrow came, the emancipation and divorce occur, and the husband does not have the right of return; because the occurrence of emancipation is simultaneous with the occurrence of divorce, so the divorce occurs while she is a slave-woman, contrary to the first case. [1] His statement: 'And divorcing her' — this is from the husband, who conditioned the divorce of his slave-wife upon the arrival of tomorrow, whether this conditioning was before the master's conditioning, after it, or with it. [2] His statement: 'no' — meaning he does not have the right of return, for she becomes separated in this case with a severe irrevocable separation such that he has no right of divorce over her. [3] His statement: 'contrary to Muhammad' — this is according to the narration of Abu Hafs al-Kabir; but according to the narration of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, there is no disagreement in the issue. [4] His statement: 'occur' — due to the existence of the condition of emancipation and divorce, which is the arrival of tomorrow. In summary: If the condition upon which the master conditioned emancipation and upon which the husband conditioned divorce is the same, they both occur upon the existence of the condition together. [5] His statement: 'and he does not have' — meaning he cannot return to her during her waiting period, because return is only permissible after two divorces for a free woman, but for a slave-woman there is no return except after one divorce; and two divorces make her irreversibly separated and not lawful for him until she marries another husband, like a free woman after three divorces. Here, since emancipation and divorce occurred together, no right of return remains, because it is only possible if the divorce occurs while she is in a state of freedom. [6] His statement: 'contrary to the first case' — which is where he said: 'You are divorced twice with (ma'a) the emancipation of your master', and then the master emancipates; in that case the two divorces occur and he has the right of return, because the occurrence of divorce is dependent on emancipation, so emancipation is made prior and divorce subsequent, and thus the divorce occurs in her state of freedom. But here it is not so, for the husband did not mention the master's emancipation in his condition, neither as a condition nor as a conjunction, but rather conditioned it upon the arrival of tomorrow, regardless of the master's emancipation or lack thereof. So emancipation is not made a condition for its occurrence such that its precedence is considered. And the situation is that the master also conditioned her emancipation upon the same condition that the husband conditioned upon, so both occur simultaneously upon the existence of the condition, without regard to precedence or subsequence.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi disputer regarding a case of conditional emancipation and divorce. The author (al-Sadr al-Shahid? Actually it's from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah) comments on a ruling from Sharh al-Wiqayah. The core issue: If a master says to his slave-woman 'If tomorrow comes, you are free,' and the husband separately says 'If tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice,' then when tomorrow arrives, both the emancipation and the divorce occur simultaneously. The husband does not have the right to take her back (raj'a) because the divorce happened while she was still a slave (since emancipation and divorce are simultaneous, she was not yet free at the moment of divorce). According to the Hanafi ruling, a slave-woman becomes irrevocably separated after two divorces, while a free woman after three. Here, two divorces occurred while she was a slave, so she becomes irrevocably separated (bainuna mughallaza). The disagreement: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani is reported to differ on some narrations (Abu Hafs's narration says he disagrees, while Abu Sulayman's narration says no disagreement). The passage also addresses a potential objection based on the word 'ma'a' (with). In another case from al-Jami', if a man says 'You are divorced with your marriage,' it is not valid and not considered conditional. But here the context is different: the husband has the power to divorce conditionally, so his use of 'ma'a' must be interpreted as conditional. In the first case scenario (where he says 'with the emancipation of your master'), the divorce occurs after emancipation, so it is valid and he retains the right of return. The distinction is that in the present case, the husband conditions divorce solely on time, not on the master's act, so both conditions are satisfied simultaneously.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement that links a legal effect (like divorce or emancipation) to the occurrence of a future event.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period after a revocable divorce without a new marriage contract.
البينونة المغلظة (al-baynūnah al-mughallaẓah): Irrevocable separation that prevents remarriage unless the woman marries another man and is divorced (tahlil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why does the husband lose the right of return (raj'a) when he conditions double divorce on the same future event as the master's emancipation?
A) Because the divorce is invalid if the wife becomes free at the same time.
B) Because the divorce occurs while the wife is still a slave, and two divorces for a slave-woman cause irrevocable separation.
C) Because the master's emancipation takes priority and nullifies the divorce.
D) Because the husband did not intend to divorce her after freedom.

Key Takeaway
When two distinct conditional acts (emancipation and divorce) share the same independent condition, they occur simultaneously; the divorce takes effect on the wife's status at that moment, and if she is a slave, two divorces are irrevocable.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Simultaneous Conditional Divorce and Manumission: No Right of Raj'a
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on simultaneous conditional divorce and manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on raj'a when divorce and manumission are conditional on the same event, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
When divorce and manumission are conditional on the same event, both occur simultaneously; the husband cannot take back his slave wife because two divorces while she is still a slave create an irrevocable separation.

This lesson explores a specific case of conditional divorce and manumission. A master and husband independently suspend the manumission and divorce of a slave woman on the same future event. When the event occurs, a critical question arises: does the husband have the right to take back his wife (raj'a)? The Hanafi school holds he does not, but Imam Muhammad (according to one narration) disagrees. Understanding the reasoning behind this dispute reveals deeper principles about the relationship between conditions, timing, and the nature of divorce and freedom in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their conditional pronouncements
Note the master suspends manumission, and the husband suspends divorce twice, both on the same condition (e.g., 'If tomorrow comes').
2
Determine the timing of the events
When the condition is fulfilled, both manumission and divorce are triggered. The key question is whether one occurs before the other or simultaneously.
3
State the majority Hanafi position
The majority holds that both occur simultaneously. Since the divorce occurs while she is still a slave, two divorces make her irrevocably separated (bainuna mughallaza), so no raj'a.
4
Present Imam Muhammad's opposing view
According to one narration, Imam Muhammad argues that manumission occurs first because it is a return to the natural state and thus quicker, so the divorce happens after she is free, allowing raj'a (since a free woman can be taken back after two divorces).
5
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The rebuttal states that if no temporal priority was considered in the pronouncements, it cannot be imposed on their effects. The condition is identical, so the events coincide.
Thamarah al-khilaf: the practical difference is whether the husband can take back his wife.

الغد
[١] قوله : وتطليقها ؛ هذا من الزوج بأن علّق طلاق امرأته الأمة بمجيء سواءً كان هذا التعليق قبل التعليق للولي أو بعده أو معه. [۲] قوله : لا ؛ أي لا يراجع، فإنّها تبين في هذه الصورة بينونة مغلظة لا يبقى معها ملك طلاق له عليها. [٣]قوله : خلافاً لمحمد الله ؛ هذا على رواية أبي حفص الكبير ، وأما على رواية أبي سليمان الجوزجاني الله فلا خلاف في المسألة. ذلك
[0] قوله : ولا يملك أي لا تجوز له الرجعة في العدة، وذلك لأن الرجعة إنما تجوز بعد اثنين في الحرّة، وأمّا الأمة فلا رجعة فيها إلا بعد واحد، والاثنان تصير به بائنة بينونة مغلظة لا تحل له حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره، كالحرة بعد ثلاث ، وهاهنا لما وقع العتق والطلاق معاً لم يبق ملك الرجعة، فإنّه إنما يمكن إذا وقع الطلاق حالة حريتها. [٦]قوله : بخلاف المسألة الأولى ؛ وهي ما إذا قال : أنت طالق ثنتين مع عتق مولاك، فأعتق المولى حيث يقعُ الاثنان ويملك الرجعة ؛ لأنَّ وقوع الطلاق هناك مرتب على العتق ، فجعل العنق مقدماً والطلاق مؤخراً، فيكون الطلاق حالة حريتها.
وأما هاهنا فليس كذلك، فإنّ الزوج لم يذكر في تعليقه عتق المولى، لا على سبيل الشرط، ولا على سبيل المعيّة، بل علقه بمجيء الغد، مع قطع النظر عن عتقِ المولى وعدمه، فلا يجعل العتق هاهنا شرطاً لوقوعه، حتى يعتبر تقدمه، والحالُ أنَّ المولى علق عتقها أيضاً بذلك الشرط الذي علق به الزوج، فيقعان جميعاً عند وجودِ الشرط من غير لحاظ التقدم والتأخر.

فإنّ وقوع الطلاق متوقف على وقوع العتق ، فاعتبر التَّقدُّمُ والتَّأخُرُ بالرتبة. وعند محمد الله يملك الرَّجعة ؛ لأن العتق أسرع وقوعاً ؛ لأنه رجوع إلى الحالة الأصلية، وهي أمر مستحسن بخلاف الطلاق، فإنه أبغض المباحات ، فيكونُ في وقوعِهِ بُطْءٌ وتأخر.

[١] قوله : بالرتبة ؛ لأنّ العتق شرطٌ ، والشرط مقدم على المشروط رتبة، وهذا التقدم والتأخُرُ الرتبي أوجب التقدم والتأخر الزماني.
[٢] قوله : لأن العتق ... الخ ؛ حاصل دليله أنّ الذي علق به الطلاق والعتق وإن كان واحداً من غير اعتبار التقدّم والتأخر ، لكن العتق أسرع وقوعاً ؛ لكونه رجوعاً إلى الحالة الفطرية وهي الحرية، والطلاق أبغض المباحات، فيكون بطيء الوقوع، فيتقدّم العتق وقوعاً، ويقع الطلاق وهي حرّة، ونحن نقول : لما لم يعتبر التقدم والتأخر في الإيقاعين كيف يعتبر أن في الوقوعين وهما أثرهما.
[٣] قوله : أبغض ؛ كذا ورد به الحديث على ما مرّ ذكره في موضعه.
And divorcing her upon its coming is not (valid for raj'a), contrary to Muhammad. [Commentary:] It means if the master said: 'If tomorrow comes, you are free,' and the husband said: 'If tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice,' then tomorrow came, the manumission and divorce occur, and the husband does not have the right of raj'a because the occurrence of manumission is simultaneous with the occurrence of divorce, so the divorce occurs while she is a slave, contrary to the first case. [1] He said: 'And divorcing her' – this is from the husband by suspending the divorce of his slave wife upon the coming (of tomorrow), whether this suspension was before the guardian's suspension, after it, or with it. [2] He said: 'Not' – i.e., he does not take her back, for she becomes irrevocably separated in this situation, and he retains no power of divorce over her. [3] He said: 'Contrary to Muhammad' – this is according to the narration of Abu Hafs al-Kabir; as for the narration of Abu Sulayman al-Juzajani, there is no dispute in the matter. [4] He said: 'Occurs' – because the condition of manumission and divorce exists, which is the coming of tomorrow. In summary: when what the master suspended manumission upon and what the husband suspended divorce upon are the same, both occur together upon the existence of the condition. [5] He said: 'And he does not possess' – i.e., raj'a is not permissible for him during the waiting period, because raj'a is only permissible after two (divorces) for a free woman, but for a slave woman there is no raj'a except after one; two make her irrevocably separated, not permissible for him until she marries another husband, like a free woman after three. Here, since manumission and divorce occurred together, the right of raj'a remains, for it would only be possible if the divorce occurred while she was free. [6] He said: 'Contrary to the first case' – which is when he said: 'You are divorced twice upon the manumission of your master,' then the master manumits, where the two (divorces) occur and he possesses raj'a because the occurrence of divorce there is dependent on manumission, so manumission is considered prior and divorce subsequent, thus the divorce occurs while she is free. But here it is not so, because the husband did not mention the master's manumission in his suspension, neither as a condition nor as a concomitant, but rather suspended it upon the coming of tomorrow, regardless of the master's manumission or its absence. Thus manumission is not considered a condition for its occurrence here, so that its precedence is considered, whereas the master also suspended her manumission upon the same condition that the husband suspended upon, so both occur upon the existence of the condition without considering precedence or posteriority. Then (the text continues): For the occurrence of divorce is dependent on the occurrence of manumission, so precedence and posteriority are considered in rank. According to Muhammad, he possesses raj'a because manumission is quicker in occurrence; because it is a return to the original state, which is something commendable, unlike divorce, for it is the most disliked of permissible things, so its occurrence is slow and delayed. [1] He said: 'In rank' – because manumission is a condition, and the condition is prior to the conditioned in rank, and this rank-based priority necessitates temporal priority and posteriority. [2] He said: 'Because manumission... etc.' – The gist of his proof is that although the same thing is suspended upon by both divorce and manumission without considering priority and posteriority, manumission is quicker in occurrence because it is a return to the natural state of freedom, and divorce is the most disliked of permissible things, so it is slow to occur; thus manumission precedes in occurrence, and divorce occurs while she is free. And we say: Since they did not consider priority and posteriority in the pronouncements, how can they consider it in the occurrences which are their effects? [3] He said: 'Most disliked' – thus the hadith has come, as mentioned in its place.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad according to one narration) and Imam Muhammad (according to another narration) regarding a specific case of conditional divorce and manumission. The scenario: a master suspends the manumission of his slave woman on the condition that tomorrow comes, and the husband independently suspends divorcing her twice on the same condition. When tomorrow arrives, both conditions are met. The majority ruling (supported by the text) is that both the manumission and the divorce occur simultaneously, and the husband cannot take her back (raj'a) because the divorce occurs while she is still a slave, making it an irrevocable separation (bainuna mughallaza) after two divorces. The reason is that a slave woman's waiting period allows raj'a only after one divorce, not after two. Imam Muhammad's position (according to the narration of Abu Hafs) is that the husband does have raj'a. His reasoning: manumission is a return to the natural state of freedom and is therefore quicker in occurrence (being something beloved), while divorce is the most disliked of permissible things, so it is slower. Thus, even though both are suspended on the same condition, manumission occurs first in time, making the divorce occur after she free. As a free woman, two divorces do not make her irrevocably separated (she can be taken back until the third). The text then provides a rebuttal: since no temporal precedence was considered in the pronouncements, it cannot be considered in their effects. The condition is the same, so both occur at once. The commentary also distinguishes this case from an earlier one where the husband's divorce was conditional on the master's manumission explicitly; there, the divorce depends on manumission, so it occurs after freedom. Here, the husband's condition is independent. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the husband has no raj'a.

Key Terms
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The husband's right to take back his divorced wife during her waiting period without a new marriage contract, as long as it is not a third or irrevocable divorce.
بينونة مغلظة (baynunah mughallazah): An irrevocable separation that makes the wife unlawful to the husband until she marries another man and that subsequent marriage is consummated and then ends.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a divorce or manumission upon the fulfillment of a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario from the lesson, why does the husband not have the right to raj'a according to the majority Hanafi view?
A) Because the wife becomes free before the divorce.
B) Because the divorce occurs while she is still a slave, making two divorces irrevocable.
C) Because the husband's pronouncement is invalid if not made in the presence of witnesses.
D) Because the master's manumission overrides the divorce.

Key Takeaway
When divorce and manumission are independently conditional on the same event, they occur simultaneously; the husband cannot take back his slave wife because the divorce occurs before freedom, creating an irrevocable separation.

Iddah of a Conditionally Manumitted and Divorced Slave Woman
Ruling on the iddah of a conditionally manumitted and divorced slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for the iddah of a slave woman who is conditionally manumitted and divorced, and explain why the precautionary principle (ihtiyat) applies.

Core Idea
When divorce and manumission are tied to the same condition, she observes the free woman's iddah—precaution overrides default status.

In the previous case, a master suspends both the manumission and divorce of his slave woman upon the coming of tomorrow. When the condition is met, we must determine her iddah. The default would suggest a slave's iddah (two menstrual cycles), but the Hanafi school rules that she must observe a free woman's iddah (three cycles) by precaution. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this seemingly stricter ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's situation
She is a slave woman whose master said: 'When tomorrow comes, you are free and you are divorced.' Both events are suspended on the same condition.
2
Recall default iddah rules
A free woman's iddah for divorce is three menstrual cycles (quru'); a slave woman's iddah is two cycles. This is the baseline.
The iddah of a free woman is longer to allow more time for reconciliation and to ensure no pregnancy from the prior marriage.
3
Understand the potential timing conflict
When both manumission and divorce occur at the same moment (upon the coming of tomorrow), there is a question: did the divorce take place while she was still a slave, or after she became free? According to Imam Muhammad, manumission occurs first, so the divorce happens when she is already free.
Imam Muhammad's view is based on the idea that the condition (coming of tomorrow) brings both simultaneously, but manumission is considered to take effect immediately, before the divorce.
4
Apply the principle of ihtiyat (precaution)
Since it is possible that the divorce occurred after manumission (making her free), we take the cautious route and require the free woman's iddah. This ensures that if she indeed was free, her iddah is valid and complete.
Ihtiyat is used when there is uncertainty (shakk) about a legal status, preferring the stricter ruling to guarantee compliance.
5
Note the consensus among Hanafi scholars
The text says 'bil-ittifaq' (by consensus), meaning all Hanafi authorities agree on this ruling. It is not disputed.
Even though there is a difference of opinion about the order of events (a minority view might argue the opposite), the precautionary ruling is unanimously adopted.

Source Text

[6] He said: 'By precaution' – due to the possibility that the divorce occurred while she was free.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that addresses the waiting period (iddah) of the slave woman in the preceding case. Although she may have been a slave at the time both conditions were met, the precautionary principle dictates that she observe the iddah of a free woman, which is three menstrual cycles. The reason for this precaution is the possibility that according to one view (Imam Muhammad's), the manumission occurred first and the divorce after she was free. Since there is a valid possibility that she became free before the divorce, her iddah should be that of a free woman to be safe. This ruling is by consensus (bil-ittifaq) among the Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
العدة (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband, during which she cannot remarry. For a free woman, it is three menstrual cycles; for a slave, it is two.
الاحتياط (al-ihtiyat): Precaution; preferring a stricter ruling when there is uncertainty to ensure the validity of the act.
بالإتفاق (bil-ittifaq): By consensus; indicates that all major authorities in the school agree on the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When there is uncertainty whether a divorced slave woman was free at the time of divorce, the precautionary principle requires her to observe the longer iddah (three cycles) to ensure its validity.

The Validity of Saying 'I am Baa'in from You' as Divorce
Ruling on the effectiveness of the phrase 'anta minni baa'in' as divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the phrases 'ana minka baa'in' and 'ana minka haram' constitute a valid divorce and explain the linguistic basis.

Core Idea
Words of separation or prohibition addressed to one's spouse effect divorce because they remove the marital bond.

In Hanafi fiqh, divorce can be effected not only by explicit words like 'anti taliq' but also by metaphorical expressions (kinayah) when intended. This lesson examines a specific kinayah: the husband saying 'I am baa'in (separate) from you' or 'I am haram (forbidden) from you.' The source passage explains the linguistic and legal reasoning behind the validity of these phrases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its ruling
The text states that saying 'anta minni baa'in' or 'ana minka baa'in' / 'ana minka haram' constitutes a valid divorce.
Focus on the specific wording used.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
The word 'ibaanah' means removal of connection, rooted in 'baw'n' (separation). Similarly, 'tahrim' means prohibition.
These root meanings make the words appropriate for divorce.
3
Recognise the legal symmetry
Since marriage creates a connection and prohibition mutual between spouses, these words can be attributed to either spouse. So when a husband says 'I am baa'in from you,' it attributes separation to himself in relation to his wife, effectively divorcing her.
The mutual nature justifies the reverse attribution.
4
Confirm the madhhab position
This is the standard Hanafi view, treating such phrases as kinayah that require intent but are clear in this context.
The commentary cites al-Tabyin and al-Nahr for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويقع بأنا منك بائن
[۷]
[٧] قوله : ويقع بأنا منك بائن... الخ، الوجه في ذلك على ما في «التبيين» (۱) و«النهر (۲) وغيرهما : إنّ الإبانة لفظها مستعمل لإزالة الوصلة الثابتة بالنكاح ، من البون وهو الفصل ، وكذا لفظ التحريم، والوصلة والتحريم مشتركان بين الزوجين ، فتصح إضافة هذين اللفظين إلى كلّ منهما ، فيقع الطلاق بقوله : أنا منك حرام أو بائن.
(And it occurs (as divorce) by his saying: 'I am baa'in from you' [7] He said: 'And it occurs by saying you are baa'in from me... etc.' The reasoning according to what is in al-Tabyin and al-Nahr and others: The word ibanah (separation) is used to remove the connection established by marriage, from al-bawn which means separation; similarly the word tahrim (prohibition). And connection and prohibition are shared between the two spouses, so it is valid to attribute these two words to each of them. Thus divorce occurs by his saying 'I am haram or baa'in from you.'
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that the phrase 'I am baa'in from you' or 'I am haram from you' constitutes a valid divorce. The commentary explains the linguistic and legal basis: the word 'ibaanah' (from which 'baa'in' is derived) means to remove the connection of marriage, and 'tahrim' (prohibition) similarly indicates the removal of marital relationship. Since the marital bond and its prohibition are mutual between husband and wife, these words can be attributed to either spouse. Therefore, when a husband says to his wife 'I am baa'in (irrevocably separate) from you' or 'I am haram (forbidden) from you,' this is understood as a pronouncement of divorce, and it takes effect as such. This ruling is based on the standard Hanafi principles of divorce by clear words (sarih) and metaphorical words (kinayah). These words are considered kinayah that are clear in context.

Key Terms
بائن (baa'in): Irrevocably separated; a type of divorce that ends the marriage immediately and does not allow raj'a (taking back) without a new contract.
الإبانة (al-ibanah): Linguistically meaning separation; in legal terms, it refers to the removal of the marital bond through divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does saying 'I am haram (forbidden) from you' constitute a valid divorce in the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the word 'forbidden' implies prohibition of marital relations, and it is a kinayah for divorce.
B) No, because 'haram' is not a word specifically used for divorce.
C) Yes, but only if the husband says it three times.
D) No, because it is a statement about himself, not about his wife.

Key Takeaway
The phrases 'ana minka baa'in' and 'ana minka haram' are valid kinayah for divorce because they linguistically imply removal of the marital bond, and since the bond is mutual, these words can be attributed to either spouse.

Iddah of a Slave-Woman and Divorce by 'Ana Mink Ba'in'
Ruling on slave-woman's iddah and validity of divorce by 'ba'in'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the iddah ruling for a slave-woman in a suspended divorce and explain why the phrase 'ana mink ba'in' effects a valid ba'in divorce.

Core Idea
Slave-woman observes three menstrual periods like a free woman; 'ana mink ba'in' is a valid divorce.

This passage from the supercommentary addresses two important rulings: the precautionary iddah of a slave-woman whose divorce and emancipation are suspended on the same condition, and the effect of using the phrase 'ana mink ba'in' (I am separated from you) to effect a divorce. We will examine the legal reasoning behind these rulings and note an intra-Hanafi difference concerning the husband's right of return (raj'a).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
Read the first few lines: 'And she observes iddah like a free woman, and it falls by I am from you ba'in.' Note that there are two distinct rulings: one about the iddah of a slave-woman, the other about the validity of a particular divorce phrase.
2
Understand the precaution in the iddah
The iddah is three menstrual periods (like a free woman) even though a slave-woman normally observes two periods. This is by precaution (ihtiyat) because the divorce might occur after she becomes free if emancipation takes effect first.
This ruling is by agreement (bil-ittifaq).
3
Analyze the effect of 'ana mink ba'in'
The phrase uses 'ibana' (separation), which removes the marital tie. Since the tie is shared, the phrase is valid for either spouse to use, and if intended as divorce, it effects a ba'in divorce.
Similar to saying 'haram'.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Muhammad held that the husband retains the right of return (raj'a) because emancipation occurs faster than divorce. However, the majority disagree, arguing that suspension did not sequence the occurrences, so they happen simultaneously or divorce occurs first. The mufta bihi is that divorce occurs, and she becomes ba'in.
The debate hinges on whether rank (rutba) can create temporal sequence.
5
Conclude the operative rulings
For practice: a slave-woman in such a suspended case must wait three menstrual periods; and a husband uttering 'ana mink ba'in' with intent effects a single irrevocable divorce.
Both rulings are established in the zahir al-riwayah.

And she observes iddah like a free woman, and it falls (the divorce) by 'I am from you ba'in'. For the occurrence of divorce is dependent on the occurrence of emancipation, so precedence and delay are considered by rank. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), he has the right of return (raj'a), because emancipation occurs more quickly; because it is a return to the original state, and it is an approved matter, unlike divorce, for it is the most detestable of permissible things, so in its occurrence there is slowness and delay. "And she observes iddah like a free woman" by agreement, taking precaution. "And it falls by 'I am from you ba'in'" [1] He said: 'by rank'; because emancipation is a condition, and the condition precedes the conditioned in rank, and this rank-based precedence and delay necessitated temporal precedence and delay. [2] He said: 'because emancipation...'; the gist of his evidence is that that to which both divorce and emancipation are suspended, although it is one thing without considering precedence or delay, yet emancipation occurs more quickly because it is a return to the natural state, which is freedom, and divorce is the most detestable of permissible things, so it is slow to occur; thus emancipation occurs first, and divorce falls while she is free. And we say: when precedence and delay were not considered in the two suspensions, how can they be considered in the two occurrences, which are their effects? [3] He said: 'most detestable'; thus it is reported in the hadith as mentioned in its place. [4] He said: 'and she observes iddah'; i.e., that slave-woman whose divorce and emancipation were suspended upon the coming of tomorrow. [5] He said: 'like a free woman'; i.e., like the iddah of a free woman, which is three menstrual periods. [6] He said: 'by precaution'; due to the possibility of the divorce occurring in a state of freedom. [7] He said: 'and it falls by 'I am from you ba'in'...'; the point according to what is in al-Tabyin and al-Nahr and others: the word 'ibana' (separation) is used to remove the connection established by marriage, from 'al-bayn' which means separation, and likewise the word 'tahrim' (prohibition). The connection and prohibition are shared between the spouses, so the addition of these two words to each of them is valid, so divorce falls by his saying: 'I am from you haram or ba'in'.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from the supercommentary addresses two rulings: first, the iddah of a slave-woman whose divorce and emancipation are both suspended on a future event (e.g., 'if tomorrow comes, you are free and divorced'). By agreement of the Hanafi school, she observes iddah like a free woman (three menstrual periods) out of precaution, since the divorce might actually occur while she is free if the emancipation precedes the divorce in time. The second ruling is that saying 'I am from you ba'in' (I am separated from you) does effect divorce if the husband intends it. The reason is that the word 'ba'in' and 'haram' are commonly used to remove the marital tie and can be addressed to either spouse, unlike the word 'talaq' which is specifically for the wife. The early Hanafis differed: Muhammad al-Shaybani held that the husband retains the right of return (raj'a) because emancipation occurs faster than divorce, but the relied-upon position is that divorce occurs immediately and she is fully separated. The commentary also refutes the argument that because the suspension did not consider sequence, the occurrence should not consider sequence either; the author of the hashia contends that the two are different.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): A divorce that effects a complete separation, after which the husband cannot remarry the wife without a new marriage and her marriage to another man.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before she can remarry, here specified as three menstrual periods for a free woman.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; a meritorious act that is quicker in occurrence than divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Saying 'You Are Divorced Once or Not'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on doubt in divorce pronouncements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the phrase 'you are divorced once or not', explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and apply the rule to similar doubt-introducing phrases.

Core Idea
Doubt on the number casts doubt on the original pronouncement—so no divorce occurs.

This passage examines a subtle but consequential dispute within the Hanafi school. When a husband says 'you are divorced once or not', does the doubt introduced by 'or not' prevent any divorce from taking effect? Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf say yes, while Imam Muhammad and early Abu Yusuf hold that the doubt only affects the number, so one divorce still occurs. We will trace the reasoning behind each position and see which is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase in dispute
Locate the phrase 'you are divorced once or not' (أنت طالق واحدة أو لا). Note the structure: a divorce pronouncement followed by a number and the disjunctive 'or not'.
2
Understand the role of doubt
The word 'or' introduces doubt (shakk) as to whether the number is affirmed or negated. Identify whether this doubt affects the number only or the original pronouncement.
The key question: does the doubt 'infect' the original act of divorce?
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
According to Abu Hanifa, when a description (number) is coupled with the original, the occurrence is by the number. Therefore, doubt on the number causes doubt on the original occurrence itself, so no divorce falls.
The principle: al-wasf idha qurina bi al-‘adad kana al-wuqu‘ bi al-‘adad.
4
Analyze Muhammad's reasoning
Imam Muhammad argues that the doubt only affects the number; the original pronouncement 'you are divorced' remains certain, so one revocable divorce takes effect.
Muhammad treats the number as an independent element, not integral to the occurrence.
5
Note the thamarah and tarjih
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, no divorce; under Muhammad, one revocable divorce. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that nothing occurs, as it is the view of Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عليك حرام إن نَوَى، لا بأنا منك طالق وإن نوى، وأنتِ طالق واحدة أو لا، أو مع موتي
نعم يفرق بينهما بأن في الثانية لا يحتاج إلى لفظ : منّي ؛ لأنه إذا أضيف هذا اللفظ وما شاكله تعيّن لإزالة ما بينهما من الوصلة والحلّ، ويحتاج الأولى إلى زيادة منك ؛ الجواز أن تكون له امرأة أخرى، فيريد بقوله : أنا بائن منها أو حرام عليها، وهذا كله بخلاف الطلاق، فإنّ محله هو المرأة لا الرجل، وبهذا لو ملكها الطلاق فطلّقته لا يقع شيء، فلهذا لا يقعُ شيء بقوله : أنا منك طالق ، وإن نوى به الطلاق.
[١]قوله : إن نوى ؛ إنما احتيج إلى النية ؛ لعدم كون هذه الألفاظ صريحة للطلاق.
[٢]قوله : لا ؛ أي لا يقع شيء، سواء نوى الطلاق أو لم ينو، ولو قال : أنا بريء من نكاحك يقع إن نوى، ولو قال : أنا بريء من طلاقك، لم يقع. كذا في «الجوهرة النيرة» (٢).

[٤] قوله : أو مع موتى ؛ أي إذا قال : أنت طالق مع موتي، أو أنت طالق مع موتك لا يقع شيء ؛ لأنه أضاف الطلاق إلى حالة منافية له.
(1) أي فهو لغو لا يعبأ به ؛ لأن الطلاق شرع مضافاً إلى المرأة ، فإذا طلق الزوج نفسه فقد غير المشروع. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر»(١ : ٣٩٦).
(۲) «الجوهرة النيرة» (٢ : ٣٥)
(۳) «الهداية» (٤ : ٤١).
Or 'you are forbidden if he intends' – no, 'I am divorced from you' even if he intends. And 'you are divorced once or not' or 'with my death'. Yes, there is a difference between them: in the second phrase there is no need for the word 'from me', because when this word and its like are added, it is determined to remove the connection and lawfulness between them; the first phrase needs the addition of 'from you' because it is possible that he has another wife, and he means by saying 'I am ba'in from her' or 'forbidden upon her'. All this is contrary to divorce, because its place is the woman, not the man. For this reason, if she owned the right of divorce and divorced him, nothing occurs. For this, nothing occurs by his saying 'I am divorced from you' even if he intends divorce by it. [1] His saying: 'if he intends' – intention is needed because these words are not explicit for divorce. [2] His saying: 'no' – meaning nothing occurs, whether he intends divorce or not. And if he says 'I am free from your marriage', it occurs if he intends; and if he says 'I am free from your divorce', it does not occur. Thus in 'al-Jawhara al-Nayyira' (2). [3] His saying: 'And you are divorced' – meaning nothing occurs when he says to her 'you are divorced once or not'. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf later. According to Muhammad and Abu Yusuf initially, she is divorced one revocable divorce, because he introduced doubt and hesitation concerning one, due to the word 'or' between it and the negative 'not', so the attribution of one falls away, and his saying 'you are divorced' remains, so one occurs. Yes, if he said 'you are divorced or not', nothing occurs due to the existence of doubt in the original pronouncement. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): when the description is coupled with the number, the occurrence is by the number not by the original, as will come, so doubt enters from this perspective on the original divorce as well, so it does not occur. Thus in 'al-Hidaya' (3). [4] His saying: 'or with my death' – meaning if he says 'you are divorced with my death' or 'you are divorced with your death', nothing occurs because he added divorce to a state incompatible with it. (1) That is, it is idle talk not to be regarded, because divorce is prescribed as attached to the woman; so if the husband divorces himself, he has changed the prescribed. See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (1:396). (2) 'al-Jawhara al-Nayyira' (2:35). (3) 'al-Hidaya' (4:41).
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of certain allusive and conditional phrases in divorce. The author begins by listing several phrases: 'or you are forbidden if he intends', 'I am divorced from you' even with intention, 'you are divorced once or not', and 'with my death'. The commentary explains that phrases like 'you are forbidden' or 'I am ba'in' require intention because they are not explicit (sarih) words for divorce. However, 'I am divorced from you' does not effect divorce even with intention, because divorce is attached to the woman, not the man. The major dispute is over the phrase 'you are divorced once or not'. According to Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf, nothing occurs because the doubt introduced by 'or not' affects the number, not the original pronouncement, and thus no divorce falls. According to Imam Muhammad and early Abu Yusuf, one revocable divorce occurs because the doubt only affects the number, leaving the original pronouncement intact. The text ultimately sides with Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position. Additionally, adding divorce to death ('with my death') is ineffective because it attaches divorce to a state incompatible with its nature.

Key Terms
صريحة (sariha): Explicit words for divorce that do not require intention to effect divorce.
نية (niyya): Intention in the heart; required for allusive divorce phrases to make them effective.
بائن (ba'in): A type of divorce that makes the wife irrevocably separated; used in phrases like 'I am ba'in from you'.
رجعية (raj'iyya): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a husband says to his wife 'you are divorced once or not' (أنت طالق واحدة أو لا)?
A) One revocable divorce takes effect.
B) Nothing takes effect.
C) One irrevocable divorce takes effect.
D) Three divorces take effect.

Key Takeaway
When 'or not' follows a number in a divorce pronouncement, the doubt enters the original occurrence, so no divorce falls—this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Adding Divorce to a Future Time: Valid and Invalid Additions
Ruling on validity of time-attached divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity of a divorce when added to a future time and apply the rule that addition to an impossible time voids the pronouncement.

Core Idea
Adding divorce to a certain time (like death) is valid; adding to an impossible time (like before death) is void.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a key rule in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband adds a condition of time to the pronouncement of divorce, the validity depends on whether that time is certain and possible to occur. Understanding this distinction prevents invalid pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Classify the time phrase
Identify whether the phrase refers to a certain, possible time (e.g., 'with your death', 'after my death') or an impossible time (e.g., 'before my death'). Certainty and possibility are assessed objectively.
Death is a certain event, so times like 'with death' or 'after death' are possible. 'Before death' is not a fixed moment and thus impossible to pinpoint.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule of idafat al-talaq
In Hanafi fiqh, adding divorce to an uncertain condition that cannot be verified or fulfilled renders the addition invalid (batil). If the condition is certain and possible, the addition is valid and the divorce takes effect upon its occurrence.
The key is the possibility of the time actually occurring as specified.
3
Derive the ruling for each case
From the rule: 'with your death' and 'after my death' are valid; 'before my death' is invalid. The divorce is void in the latter, and no divorce occurs even if death later approaches.
The invalid addition does not convert to an immediate divorce; the pronouncement is treated as null.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مع موتك. [١]قوله : مع موتك ؛ وكذا في بعد موتي أو موتك، بخلاف قوله : قبل موتي أو موتك. (هامش: بسبب إضافته إلى حالة منافية للإيقاع أو الوقوع . ينظر : ((الدر المختار»(٢ : ٤٤٢).)
Or with your death. [1] His saying: 'with your death'; and similarly in 'after my death' or 'after your death', unlike his saying 'before my death' or 'before your death.' (Footnote: because it is added to a state that contradicts the occurrence or the falling of divorce. See: al-Durr al-Mukhtar 2:442)
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about the validity of divorce when it is attached to a future time. The phrase 'with your death' means the divorce becomes effective at the moment of death. The ruling is that it is valid because death is certain. Similarly, 'after my death' or 'after your death' are valid. However, 'before my death' or 'before your death' are invalid because the divorce is added to a time that cannot be determined or is impossible. The reason given in the footnote is that the divorce is added to a state that is incompatible with the occurrence or the falling of divorce. In Hanafi fiqh, adding divorce to an impossible condition renders the divorce invalid.

Key Terms
إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان (idafat al-talaq ila al-zaman): Attaching the occurrence of divorce to a specific time in the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is the divorce valid if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced before my death'?
A) Yes, it becomes effective immediately
B) Yes, it becomes effective before his death
C) No, it is invalid because the time cannot be determined
D) No, it is invalid because he added it to a time that will never occur

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, adding divorce to a future time is valid only if the time is certain and possible to occur; otherwise, the pronouncement is void.

No Divorce After Spousal Ownership
Ruling that divorce is invalid after ownership of spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the annulment of marriage through spousal ownership and explain why divorce cannot occur afterward.

Core Idea
Ownership of a spouse annuls the marriage; divorce is neither needed nor possible.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a unique rule in Hanafi family law: when one spouse becomes the owner of the other, the marriage is automatically dissolved. This principle rests on the incompatibility of marriage (nikah) and ownership (milk al-yamin). Understanding this helps you see the precise boundaries between different legal relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is one spouse owning the other (e.g., buying one's slave spouse). The ruling is that the marriage is annulled (batil) automatically.
The text uses 'ملك أحدهما صاحبه' — ownership of the other.
2
Locate the dalil
The author provides a logical proof (talil): 'because by one owning the other... separation occurs and the marriage is annulled, and permissibility is established by milk al-yamin.' The proof is that marriage and ownership cannot coexist.
Arabic: 'بملك أحدهما الآخر... وقعت الفرقة وانفسخ النكاح'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase 'لم يبق محل الطلاق' (no place for divorce) shows that divorce requires an existing marriage, and ownership removes that. Thus, no divorce is possible.
Divorce only occurs when marriage remains ('والطلاق إنما يقع عند بقاء النكاح').
4
Note the scope – partial ownership
The text includes 'أو شقصه' (or a share), meaning even partial ownership annuls the marriage. This broadens the application.
'شقص' means a portion or share.
5
Cross-madhhab status
All schools agree that ownership ends the marriage, but the Hanafi reasoning emphasizes automatic annulment without need for a formal divorce.
This is considered mukhtalaf fih only in the exact mechanism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا طلاق بعدما ملك أحدهما صاحبه ، أو شقصه. [٢] قوله : ولا طلاق ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا ملك أحد الزوجين الآخر، بأن كانت الزوجة أمةً لرجل فاشترى كلّها أو بعضها زوجها ، أو كان العبد زوجاً فاشترته الزوجة ، لم يبق محل الطلاق ؛ لأنّ بملك أحدهما الآخر كلاً أو بعضاً وقعت الفرقة وانفسخ النكاح ، وثبت الحل بملك اليمين والطلاق إنما يقع عند بقاء النكاح. (هامش: شقص : بكسر الشين جمعه الأشقاص : وهو الطائفة من الشيء: أي البعض. ينظر : طلبة الطلبة» (ص٢٦).)
And no divorce after one of them owns the other, or a share of him. [2] His saying: 'And no divorce...' etc.; meaning that if one of the spouses owns the other, such as if the wife is a slave woman of a man and her husband buys her entirely or partially, or if the husband is a slave and his wife buys him, there remains no place for divorce; because by one of them owning the other entirely or partially, separation occurs and the marriage is annulled, and permissibility is established by ownership of the right hand, and divorce only occurs when the marriage remains. (Footnote: Shaqs: with a kasrah on the shin, plural ashqas: a portion of something, i.e., a part. See: Tulbat al-Talaba, p. 26)
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage states a key principle in Hanafi family law: when one spouse becomes the owner of the other (for example, if the husband buys his wife who is a slave, or the wife buys her husband who is a slave), the marriage is automatically dissolved due to the ownership. This is because marriage and ownership cannot coexist in the same relationship. Once ownership is established, even partially (a share), the marriage is annulled, and divorce cannot be pronounced because divorce requires an existing marriage. The permissibility of relations is then based on 'milk al-yamin' (right hand ownership) instead of marriage.

Key Terms
ملك اليمين (milk al-yamin): Ownership of a slave, which creates permissibility of sexual relations without marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage continues as normal.
B) The marriage is annulled and no divorce is possible.
C) The marriage is suspended until the wife is freed.
D) The husband must pronounce divorce to end the marriage.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of a spouse, even partial, automatically annuls the marriage and precludes any possibility of divorce.

Counting Divorces by Finger Gestures
Ruling on counting divorces by finger gestures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on counting divorces by finger gestures and distinguish the relied-upon position from the earlier detail.

Core Idea
Spread fingers count in court; the earlier detail on hand orientation is weak and not relied upon.

When a husband says 'You are divorced like this' while pointing with his fingers, the number of divorces corresponds to the number of fingers shown. However, a nuance arises: does the orientation of the hand matter? This lesson unpacks the ruling, the initial detail, and the later stronger view that simplifies the counting in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the basic ruling
The husband says 'You are divorced like this' (hākadhā) and points with his fingers. The basic ruling is that the number of divorces equals the number of fingers he indicates. This is because the gesture clarifies the vague number.
The word 'hākadhā' is ambiguous without the gesture.
2
Locate the initial detail on hand orientation
Al-Hidayah mentions a detail: if he points with the palms of his fingers, the count is based on spread (manshurah) fingers; if he points with the backs, the count is based on closed (madhmumah) fingers.
The commentary notes that this detail is introduced with the phrase 'qīla' (it is said), indicating weakness.
3
Understand the later Hanafi position
Later scholars like al-Shurunbulali consider this detail weak. The relied-upon view is that in court, the number of spread fingers is always taken into account, regardless of hand orientation. This is based on custom (urf) and the Sunnah.
The husband's private intention (if he meant closed fingers) is accepted in private (diyānatan) but not in court.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a husband points with the back of his hand showing three spread fingers, the court rules that three divorces occurred. If he claims he intended the closed fingers (only two), he is believed in private but the court enforces three.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that the number of spread fingers governs court decisions.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage explains how divorces are counted when a husband says 'You are divorced like this' and points with his fingers. The number of divorces corresponds to the number of fingers he shows. However, there is a difference between pointing with the palms (where the spread fingers are counted) and pointing with the backs of the hand (where the closed fingers are counted). The commentary notes that this detail is disputed: some later scholars like al-Shurunbulali consider this detail weak, and maintain that regardless of how he points, the number of spread fingers is always taken into account in court, based on custom and the Sunnah. However, if he intends a different number (the closed ones), he is believed in private (between him and Allah). Thus, the later relied-upon position in the Hanafi school simplifies the ruling: in court, the number of divorces is the number of spread fingers shown, regardless of palm or back orientation.

Key Terms
المنشورة (al-manshurah): The spread fingers that are opened and extended.
المضمومة (al-madhmumah): The closed fingers that are not spread out.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when a husband divorces by pointing with fingers, the court counts the spread fingers shown, irrespective of hand orientation; his private intention is accepted only between him and Allah.

Counting Divorces by Finger Indication
Explaining the number of divorces when indicating with fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi ruling for counting divorces by finger indication and explain the difference between the earlier and later Hanafi views.

Core Idea
The number of divorces is determined by the extended fingers shown, regardless of palm orientation, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

When a husband says 'You are divorced like this' and indicates a number with his fingers, how many divorces occur? The answer depends on whether we consider the orientation of his palm or simply the number of extended fingers. This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute where later scholars simplified the earlier ruling, prioritizing custom and practical consistency. Understanding this shift reveals how Hanafi fiqh evolves to balance textual detail with real-world application.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the earlier distinction
Identify the husband's action: he says 'Anti taliq hākadhā' (you are divorced like this) and shows his fingers. The earlier view in al-Hidayah distinguishes based on palm orientation: if he shows the inner side (palm), count extended fingers; if he shows the outer side (back), count clenched fingers.
The term 'manshūrah' means extended fingers, 'madmūmah' means clenched.
2
Identify the later critique and its basis
Later scholars like Sharnablali and Ibn Abidin criticized the earlier distinction as weak ('da'īf'). They argued that custom ('urf) and the sunnah dictate that the extended fingers are what people understand, regardless of palm orientation. Therefore, the count should always be based on extended fingers.
This critique is rooted in practical consistency and avoidance of confusion.
3
Understand the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position is that the extended fingers are counted unconditionally for judicial purposes (qaḍā'an). However, if the husband privately intended the clenched fingers, his intention is accepted between him and Allah (diyānatan).
This dual approach balances public legal determination with private conscience.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
Suppose a husband shows the back of his hand with three extended fingers and says 'Anti taliq hākadhā'. Under the earlier view, since the back is shown, the number of clenched fingers (two) would be counted, resulting in two divorces. Under the mufta bihi, three extended fingers give three divorces judicially, but if he intended two, he is believed privately.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) is a difference in judicial outcome.

(And the extended are considered) if he indicates with their inner sides, and if he indicates with their outer sides, then the clenched. [1] His statement 'occurs according to its number': i.e., according to the number he indicated with of the finger: if one, then one; if two, then two; if three, then three; because indication with the finger conveys knowledge of the number customarily and in usage when combined with the ambiguous number, which is his statement 'like this'. [2] His statement 'is feminine': to counter what is said that 'finger' is among feminine words heard, so how did the author use the masculine pronoun referring to it. [3] His statement 'and is considered': meaning if he indicates with fingers by extending some, opening them, stretching them, and clenching some, then the consideration is for the number of extended if he indicates with the inner sides of the fingers, and the clenched if he indicates with their outer sides. [4] His statement 'the extended': and if he intended the clenched, he is believed privately (diyaanatan) not judicially (qada'an). [0] His statement 'their inner sides': the inner side of the finger is the surface that faces the palm, and its outer side is opposite. [6] His statement 'then the clenched': this detail was mentioned in Al-Hidayah in the form of 'qila', and Sharnablali and others mentioned that this detail is weak, and that what is considered is the extended unconditionally, according to custom and sunnah, and the clenched is considered if he intends privately.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about how many divorces occur when a husband indicates a number by showing his fingers. The matn states that the number is determined by the fingers shown, but distinguishes between when he shows the palms (inner sides) and when he shows the backs (outer sides). In the first case, the number of extended fingers is counted; in the second, the number of clenched fingers is counted. The commentary clarifies that the finger indication conveys the number by custom. However, a later view represented by Sharnablali and endorsed by Ibn Abidin considers the detailed distinction weak. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the extended fingers are always counted, regardless of palm orientation, because that is the custom and sunnah. If the husband intended the clenched fingers (e.g., he intended fewer), he is believed in private (between him and Allah) but not in court. Thus, the lesson shows a shift from an earlier detailed ruling to a simpler, more practical fatwa.

Key Terms
المنشورة (al-manshurah): The extended or spread fingers; when indicating with the palm facing the addressee, the number of extended fingers is counted.
المضمومة (al-madmumah): The closed or clenched fingers; when indicating with the back of the hand, the number of fingers that are closed is counted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view (Sharnablali), how many divorces occur if a husband says 'Anti taliq' and indicates with three extended fingers while showing the back of his hand, without any specific intention?
A) Three divorces
B) One divorce
C) Two divorces
D) It depends on whether the fingers are extended or closed

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position counts the number of extended fingers shown, regardless of palm orientation, with the husband's private intention considered only between him and Allah.

Descriptive Terms in Divorce Resulting in One Irrevocable Divorce
Listing descriptive phrases that cause one ba'in divorce unless three are intended

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of descriptive divorce phrases (such as 'bain', 'ashadd', 'talaq al-shaytan') and identify the role of intention in determining the number of divorces.

Core Idea
Descriptive phrases in divorce result in one irrevocable divorce unless the husband intends three.

Divorce can be pronounced with various descriptive terms that may seem to imply multiple divorces or severity. This passage from the matn clarifies that without explicit intention, such phrases yield only one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. The commentary provides the legal reasoning behind each type of description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and category
The act is pronouncing talaq with descriptive terms (e.g., bain, ashadd, talaq al-shaytan). These are all valid utterances of divorce.
The category is talaq, which is the dissolution of marriage.
2
Determine the default ruling
Without intention of three, all these phrases result in one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. The intention of the husband is key: if he intends three, then three occur; otherwise, one.
Intention is considered when the phrase is ambiguous.
3
Trace the reasoning for each phrase type
For 'bain': the word itself specifies irrevocability. For 'ashadd' and similar superlatives: they describe the effect (immediate finality). For 'talaq al-shaytan' etc.: these refer to prohibited divorce, which in Hanafi is any divorce other than single revocable. For similes like 'like a mountain': they are ambiguous, so minimum applies. For adjectives like 'severe' etc.: they describe difficulty, which matches ba'in.
The reasoning is based on common usage and legal effect.
4
Note the exception
If the husband says 'bi-aktharihi' (with the most) or explicitly intends three, then three divorces occur.
The phrase 'bi-aktharihi' is a definitive expression of number.
5
Apply to hypothetical scenarios
Practice applying the ruling: for each phrase, state whether it results in one ba'in or three, based on the absence or presence of intention.
This step solidifies the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : أشدّ الطلاق ؛ وكذا أشرّ الطلاق، وأسوأ الطلاق، أو أخشنه ، أو أكبره، أو أعظمه، أو أطوله ، أو أغلظه ، أو أعرضه، وبأكثره يقع الثلاث. كذا في «البحر»(۱)
