(٤) قوله : واحدة بائنة ؛ بالرفع أي تقعُ واحدة بائنة بهذه الألفاظ كلّها، والوجه في ذلك على ما في «الهداية وشروحها : إنّ في قوله : أنت طالق بائن، وصفه بالبينونة ، والطلاق يحتمله ألا ترى إلى أنه تحصل به البينونة في غير المدخولة، وفي المدخولة بعد العدة، فيكون هذا الوصف تعييناً لأحد محتمليه.
وفي قوله : «أفحش الطلاق» ؛ لأنه إنّما يوصف الطلاق بهذا الوصف باعتبار أثره، وهو البينونة في الحال.
وقس عليه : أخبث الطلاق وأسوأه ونحو ذلك.
وفي قوله : «طلاق الشيطان أو البدعة ؛ لأنَّ الطلاق المنهي الشرعي هو الواحد الرجعي كما مرّ، والبائن بدعي وشيطاني.
وفي صورة توصيفه بالأشديّة أو الشدّة أو الطول أو العرض؛ لأنه يقال : الأشدّ لما يصعب عليه، وكذا الطويلُ العريض، وإن هو إلا البائن.
And 'You are divorced irrevocably, or you are divorced the most severe of divorce, or the most obscene, or the most wicked, or the divorce of Satan, or of innovation, or like a mountain, or like a thousand, or filling the house, or a severe divorce, or long, or wide' - without intention of three, one irrevocable [divorce]. [1] His statement 'in the side'; and then the indication is with the inner sides of the fingers. [2] His statement 'the most severe of divorce'; and likewise the most evil, the worst, the roughest, the greatest, the biggest, the longest, the harshest, the widest, and with 'the most' three occur. This is in Al-Bahr. [3] His statement 'like a thousand'; the similitude to it may be in intensity or in number, so if he did not intend three, the lesser is established, which is the one irrevocable. (4) His statement 'one irrevocable'; in the nominative, i.e., one irrevocable occurs with all these phrases. The reasoning for that according to Al-Hidayah and its commentaries: In his statement 'You are divorced irrevocably', it is a description with irrevocability, and divorce can accommodate it; do you not see that divorce effects irrevocability in the one not consummated, and in the one consummated after the waiting period? So this description specifies one of its possibilities. And in his statement 'the most obscene of divorce': because divorce is described with this description considering its effect, which is immediate irrevocability. And analogize to it: the most wicked of divorce, the worst, and similar. And in his statement 'divorce of Satan or innovation': because the legally prohibited divorce is the single revocable, as previously, and the irrevocable is innovative and satanic. And in the case of describing it with severity or intenseness or length or width: because it is said that the severe is what is difficult upon him, and likewise the long and wide, and it is none other than the irrevocable.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn, with explanatory reasoning from the commentary. It lists descriptive phrases a husband might use when pronouncing divorce, such as 'bain' (irrevocable), 'ashadd al-talaq' (most severe), 'talaq al-shaytan' (satanic divorce), etc. The ruling is that if the husband does not intend three divorces, all these phrases result in one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. The commentary explains the reasoning: for 'bain', the word itself specifies irrevocability, which is one possible effect of divorce. For 'ashadd' and similar superlatives, they describe the effect (immediate finality) rather than the number. For 'talaq al-shaytan or al-bid'a', these terms refer to prohibited divorce, which in the Hanafi school is the revocable one? Wait, the commentary says 'the legally prohibited divorce is the single revocable, as previously, and the ba'in is innovative and satanic.' This is a bit nuanced: the sunnah divorce is a single revocable divorce, so anything else is bid'a. Thus calling it shaytani or bid'a implies it is the ba'in (irrevocable) because that goes against the sunnah. For comparisons like 'like a mountain' or 'like a thousand', they are ambiguous between strength and number, so the minimum (one ba'in) is applied. Finally, adjectives like 'severe', 'long', 'wide' are interpreted as referring to the irrevocable type because it is more difficult for the husband. The lesson guards against the misconception that these expressions automatically mean three divorces; they only mean three if the husband explicitly intends three.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that immediately ends the marriage; the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.
الطلاق الشيطاني (al-talaq al-shaytani): A divorce that is prohibited (makruh tahrimi); in this context, it is interpreted as the ba'in divorce because the sunnah divorce is revocable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Anti taliq ashadd al-talaq' without any intention, what is the effect?
A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, descriptive terms of severity, type, or similitude in divorce result in a single irrevocable divorce unless the husband explicitly intends a higher number.

Talaq — Divorce

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Simile and Descriptive Divorce Phrases
Intra-Hanafi dispute on simile and descriptive divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi positions on simile divorce and state the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position with its dalil.

Core Idea
Any simile in divorce indicates bain — but the Hanafi imams differ on whether the object's greatness matters.

When a husband uses a description or simile in his divorce statement (e.g., 'You are divorced like a mountain' or 'the most severe divorce'), the default ruling is one bain (irrevocable) divorce unless he intends three. However, the early Hanafi masters disagreed on what exactly triggers bain: is it any simile, or only when greatness is explicit or customary? This lesson unpacks the dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar, and clarifies the operative position for fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The husband utters a divorce phrase with a description or simile. The default rule (without intention of three) is that it results in one bain divorce. Understand that bain is immediate and irrevocable.
The matn lists many examples: 'most severe divorce', 'like a mountain', 'like a thousand', etc.
2
Recognize the three intra-Hanafi positions
There are three views: (1) Abu Hanifa: any simile, even with a contemptible object like a needle's head, gives bain. (2) Abu Yusuf: only if the word 'great' (عظيم) is explicitly mentioned. (3) Zufar: only if the object of simile is commonly considered great by people. Note that Muhammad has two narrations, one with Abu Hanifa and one with Abu Yusuf.
This difference matters for phrases like 'like a needle's head' or 'like a grain of mustard'.
3
Follow the dalil for each position
The common proof is that a description of severity or a simile implies emphasis, which aligns with bain. Abu Hanifa's proof: التشبيه يقتضي زيادة (the simile necessitates increase), and this increase is independent of the object's size. Abu Yusuf argues that without the word 'great', the simile might be abstract. Zufar argues that custom restricts greatness to what people consider great.
Understand that the dispute is about the scope of the illa (effective cause).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the husband says 'You are divorced like a needle's head', according to Abu Hanifa it is one bain; according to Abu Yusuf it is not bain if 'great' is not mentioned; according to Zufar it is not bain because a needle's head is not commonly great. Similarly, for 'like the bone of a needle', Abu Yusuf would still require 'great'.
The thamarah shows the real-world effect of the difference.
5
Apply the tarjih and note the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Al-Hidayah, Al-Bahr al-Raiq) favor Abu Hanifa's view that any simile yields bain, as it gives maximum effect to the husband's words and avoids uncertainty. The mufta bihi is therefore that any simile (even with a small object) results in one bain divorce (unless three intended).
Also note that if three is intended, three divorces occur regardless of the phrase, because bain-ness can be light or severe.

(1) 'Al-Bahr al-Raiq' (3:310). [PAGE_270] 270 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya And with it three. And with it three (1) His saying: without intention of three, includes when he did not intend a number, or intended one, or two. [2] And in the cases of simile with the mountain and the like; because the simile necessitates increase, whether the simile is with a contemptible thing like the head of a needle or a great thing like a mountain. Magnificence. According to Abu Yusuf, if he mentioned 'great', it is bain, otherwise not: meaning whatever the object of simile is; because the simile may be abstract. And according to Zufar: If the object of simile is something described as great in people's view, it falls as bain, otherwise not. This is all when he did not intend anything or intended one or intended two. But if he intended three with these words, then three; because he intended one of the possibilities of his word, since the bain-ness that is the meaning of his word by description or simile is of two types. So if he intended one of them, namely the severe bain-ness, his intention is considered. He said in 'Al-Bahr' (2): The summary is that description indicating increase necessitates bain, and simile likewise: whatever the object of simile is, like the head of a needle or a grain of mustard or a sesame seed. Abu Yusuf conditioned the mention of 'great' absolutely, and Zufar conditioned that it be great in people's view. So the head of a needle is bain only in the first [view]; and like a mountain in the first and third; and like the bone of a mountain according to all; and like the bone of a needle according to the first two. And Muhammad: some say with the first, some with the second. [1] His saying: and with it three; i.e., with the intention of three, three occurs in all cases. Some mashayikh corrected that intention of three is not considered for 'severe' or 'long' or 'wide' divorce; because intention operates only on what is probable, and 'taliqah' with the tā' of the unit cannot bear three. And it was answered that the tā' may be for the feminization of the word, or it may be superfluous, so it does not harm the intention of three. Moreover, even if it is conceded that the tā' is for the unit, there is no harm either; because the consideration of the intention of three is not due to the word 'taliqah' indicating it, but rather because when he described it with a description indicating severity and difficulty, that indicates bain-ness, and it is of two types, so if he intended one of them, that is valid. [2] His saying: or two; its reasoning is what passed earlier, that two is a pure number that the word cannot bear.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on divorce phrases. The matn states that phrases like 'you are divorced with a bain divorce' or 'the most severe divorce' result in one bain divorce if no intention of three. The commentator explains that these descriptions specify the type of divorce as bain (irrevocable) immediately. For similes like 'like a mountain', the simile implies greatness/severity, so it also gives one bain. However, there is ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams: Abu Yusuf requires explicitly mentioning 'great' for the bain ruling; Zufar requires that the object of simile be considered great by people; while Abu Hanifa (and Muhammad according to some) holds that any simile, even with a small object like a needle head, yields bain because the act of similing itself indicates something extraordinary. The passage then clarifies that if three is intended, three divorces occur, because bain-ness can be either light (revocable) or severe (irrevocable), and intending three chooses the severe type. There is also a discussion about the word 'taliqah' (with tā' indicating singular) and whether it can bear three: some early mashayikh doubted it, but the answer is that the tā' may be superfluous or for feminization, and the intention works through the description, not the word itself.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without right of return except through a new marriage.
تشبيه (tashbīh): A simile used in divorce to emphasize the nature of the divorce, e.g., 'like a mountain'.
تطليقة شديدة (taṭlīqah shadīdah): The phrase 'a severe divorce' used to describe the divorce as strong or severe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'You are divorced like a needle's head' without any intention. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling?
A) It is not a valid divorce because the object is too small.
B) It is one revocable (raj'i) divorce.
C) It is one bain divorce.
D) It is three divorces because the simile implies emphasis.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position on simile divorce is that any simile, regardless of the object's size or explicit mention of 'great', results in one bain divorce (unless three is intended).

Effect of Describing Divorce with Severity and Similes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on descriptive divorce phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on how describing divorce (tashbīh) affects its finality, and apply the rule to determine the number of divorces based on intention.

Core Idea
A divorce described with severity or compared to something becomes final (bā'in) if the description indicates increase; the number depends on intention, not the description.

This passage examines a debate within the Hanafi school: does describing a divorce as 'severe' or comparing it to a mountain automatically make it a final divorce? The answer affects how many divorces occur and whether it is revocable. Understanding these positions is essential for issuing correct fatwa in cases of ambiguous divorce utterances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi (Abu Hanifa) position
Start by understanding that Abu Hanifa holds that any description indicating increase—regardless of the object—causes a final divorce (bā'in). The comparison itself implies an increase in finality, making the divorce irrevocable.
This is the general position as summarized in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
Identify the dissenting positions of Abu Yusuf and Zufar
Abu Yusuf requires explicit mention of 'great' (al-azim) for the divorce to become final; otherwise it remains revocable. Zufar requires that the object of comparison be considered great in people's estimation. These conditions limit when a description triggers finality.
Both are considered valid scholarly opinions within the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the reasoning for each position
Abu Hanifa: the comparison itself conveys an increase in severity, so finality follows. Abu Yusuf: comparison can be for abstraction (tajrīd) unless 'great' is specified. Zufar: common perception of greatness matters because tashbīh is understood in urf.
The reasoning is based on usul principles of dalalah and urf.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The text and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefer Abu Hanifa's view: any description or comparison indicating increase causes final divorce, regardless of the object. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Tarjih is based on the generality of the proof and practical considerations.
5
Apply to scenarios with intention of number
If the husband intends three divorces with such descriptions, three occur because he chose the severe type of baynūnah. If no intention, it is one final divorce. If he intends two, it is not effective because 'divorce' as a verbal noun conveys only one.
The tā' in 'tallīqah' does not prevent intending three; it may be for femininity or extra.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of describing divorce (talaq) with words that imply severity or comparing it to something (tashbih). The core issue is whether such descriptions automatically cause a final divorce (bainuna) or depend on intention. The author begins by stating that describing divorce with something that indicates increase (like 'severe,' 'long,' or comparing to a mountain) necessarily results in a final divorce, regardless of whether the comparison is to something insignificant (like a needle head) or great (like a mountain). The term 'العظمة' (al-azamah) appears as a heading, possibly meaning 'greatness' as a category. The text then presents the positions of Abu Yusuf and Zufar. Abu Yusuf says that if the word 'great' (al-azim) is explicitly mentioned, it becomes a final divorce; otherwise not, because comparison can be for abstraction. Zufar says that if the object of comparison is considered great among people, it is a final divorce; otherwise not. Both positions are limited to when the husband did not intend a specific number of divorces. If he intends three with these wordings, then three occur because he chose one of the two possible types of final divorce (light or severe). Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq summarizes that description indicating increase causes final divorce, and comparison does too, regardless of the object. Abu Yusuf requires mentioning greatness absolutely, Zufar requires it to be great in people's view. The passage then discusses the phrase 'معها ثلاث' (with it three), explaining that with the intention of three, three divorces occur in all cases. Some scholars objected that the word 'تطليقة' (a divorce) with the feminine tā' does not imply three, so intending three is ineffective. The response is that the tā' may be for feminine form or extra, and even if it indicates singularity, the intention of three is valid because the description indicates a final divorce, which can be light or severe; the husband intended the severe one. Finally, it addresses intending two: the word 'طلاق' (divorce) as a verbal noun only conveys one divorce (real or constructive), so intending two is not effective unless explicitly stated as a number.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): A final divorce that ends the marriage irrevocably, requiring a new marriage if they wish to reunite.
تشبيه (tashbīh): Comparing the divorce to something, e.g., 'like a mountain', implying a strong effect.
بينونة (baynūnah): The state of being separated after divorce; it can be light (revocable) or severe (final).
واحدة وواحدة (wāḥidatan wa wāḥidatan): Saying 'you are divorced once and once', which is interpreted as separated phrases leading to one divorce.

Triple Divorce Before Consummation
Ruling with proof on triple divorce before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on triple divorce before consummation, explain the distinction between grouped and separated divorces, and apply the ruling to free and slave women.

Core Idea
Triple divorce before consummation counts as three and requires tahlil – you cannot reclaim your wife without her marrying another man.

A common misconception is that divorcing a non-consummated wife three times results in only one divorce, because the first divorce makes her irrevocably separated. However, the Hanafi school holds that all three divorces are effective, making her permanently prohibited until tahlil. This lesson unpacks the textual proof from the Companions and the critical distinction between grouped and separated pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the exact scenario? A man divorces his wife three times before consummation. The ruling: all three divorces occur, making her permanently prohibited until tahlil.
Note that the act of pronouncing three divorces at once is makruh but the divorce itself is valid.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the fatwa of Ibn Abbas and Abu Huraira, narrated by Malik and Abu Dawud.
The hadith: 'You have sent away from your hands what was of advantage to you.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'your advantage' refers to the husband's right to take her back after one divorce. By saying three, he lost that advantage, proving all three took effect.
This is a qati' dalala (certain indication) from a zanni thubut (speculative transmission).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some early scholars and other schools hold that triple divorce does not count on a non-consummated wife.
The Hanafi position is strict and relies on the practice of the Companions.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by Zahir al-Riwayah and the fatwa of Ibn Abbas and Abu Huraira.
Any claim to the contrary is considered an error, as stated by Ibn al-Humam.

Source Text

[٢] قوله : ثلاثاً ؛ وكذا لو طلقها ثنتين مجتمعاً كقوله : أنت طالق ثنتين، وكذا لو قال : واحدة ونصفاً تقع ثنتان ؛ لأنه جملة واحدة، ولـو قـال : نصفاً وواحدة تقع واحدة. كذا في «البحر».

Book of Divorce / Simile of Divorce, its Description, and Kinayas. And whoever divorces her three times before intercourse, they fall. But if he separates, she becomes final by the first, and the second does not occur. So in: 'You are divorced once and once,' one occurs. And this is for a free woman. As for a slave woman, two take the place of three for a free woman. [1] His statement: this; meaning the occurrence of three with the intention of three, and the occurrence of one with the intention of two. [2] His statement: three; and similarly if he divorces her two together like his saying: 'You are divorced two,' and similarly if he said: 'one and a half' two occur; because it is one sentence, and if he said: 'half and one' one occurs. This is in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: they fall; meaning those three divorces. Indeed, the unpreceded woman, although she becomes final by one due to the absence of waiting period for her, but the finality there is not severe, so that it is permissible for him to marry her without tahlil. But if he divorced her three times together, they all fall, and she becomes such that she is not permissible for him until she marries another husband. This is what Ibn Abbas and Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with them, gave fatwa when asked about a man who divorced his wife before consummation. The questioner said: 'My divorce of her is only one.' Ibn Abbas said: 'You have sent away from your hands what was of advantage to you' (3). It was narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta and Abu Dawud in his Sunan. And whoever thinks that three do not occur on the unpreceded woman or that tahlil is not required when three occur, he has erred, as Ibn al-Humam elaborated in Fath al-Qadir. [4] His statement: for if he separates; it is the past tense of tafriq, and it is more general than being by sentences, like 'You are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced,' or by news, like 'You are divorced, divorced, divorced,' or by description, like 'You are divorced once, once, and once.' (1) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (3:283). (2) In al-Muwatta (2):570, and Ma'rifat al-Sunan (12:218), etc. (3) Fath al-Qadir (3:470).
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the effect of triple divorce before consummation. The author states that if a husband divorces his wife three times before consummation, all three divorces occur, making her permanently prohibited unless she marries another husband (tahlil). However, if the divorces are separated (e.g., saying 'you are divorced' three times at separate occasions or with intervening words), the first divorce takes effect immediately because she is non-consummated, and the subsequent ones do not occur because she is already irrevocably separated. The example given: if he says 'you are divorced once and once' (using 'once' twice), only one divorce is counted. This ruling applies to a free woman; for a slave woman, two divorces are equivalent to three for a free woman (i.e., two make her prohibited). The commentary then explains each part: the phrase 'هذا' (this) refers to the earlier statement that three divorces occur when intended, and one when two are intended. The word 'ثلاثاً' (three) includes cases where he says 'two' together or 'one and a half' which counts as two because it is a single sentence. The word 'وقعن' (they fall) emphasizes that all three fall, despite the common misconception that three divorces on a non-consummated wife do not count. The proof is the fatwa of Ibn Abbas and Abu Huraira, narrated by Malik and Abu Dawud, where a man who said he divorced his wife three times but later claimed he meant only one was told that he had lost his advantage (i.e., the ability to remarry without tahlil). The author warns that anyone who thinks three divorces do not occur on a non-consummated wife or that tahlil is not required is mistaken. Finally, the term 'فرق' (separated) is general, covering separation by separate sentences, by repetition of the word 'divorced', or by description like 'once, once, and once'.

Key Terms
ثلاثاً (thalāthan): Divorcing three times at once, e.g., saying 'you are divorced three times'.
فرّق (farraqa): Separating the divorces by intervals or by different phrasings so that they are not all pronounced together.
الحرَّة والأمة (al-ḥurrah wa al-amah): Free woman and slave woman; the number of divorces that cause permanent prohibition differs: three for free, two for slave.
تحليل (taḥlīl): The process of marrying another man and being divorced by him to become permissible to the first husband again after a triple divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three divorces, and she becomes permanently prohibited
D) No divorce because she is non-consummated

Key Takeaway
Triple divorce before consummation counts as three in the Hanafi school, requiring tahlil for remarriage, and the distinction between grouped and separated divorces is crucial.

Three Divorces Before Consummation: Simultaneous vs. Sequential
Ruling on three divorces before consummation: simultaneous vs. sequential effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simultaneous and sequential pronouncements of three divorces before consummation and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
Three divorces before consummation: together they all fall; sequentially only the first falls due to immediate irrevocability.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in Hanafi divorce law: the effect of pronouncing three divorces before consummation all at once versus spread out. The ruling hinges on whether the wife becomes irrevocably divorced (bain) after the first pronouncement, preventing subsequent ones from taking effect. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying divorce rulings and avoiding common errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pronouncing three divorces before consummation. The Hanafi school rules that if pronounced simultaneously (e.g., 'you are divorced three'), all three fall. If pronounced sequentially (e.g., separated by time or conjunction), only the first falls.
This applies to a free woman; for a slave woman, two divorces are equivalent to three.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the fatwa of Ibn Abbas and Abu Hurairah, recorded in Muwatta Imam Malik and Sunan Abi Dawud. When asked about a man who divorced his wife once before consummation, Ibn Abbas said, 'You have released from your hand what advantage you had,' implying the three were effective.
The hadith supports that three divorces before consummation fall completely.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase is 'You have released from your hand what advantage you had' – meaning the man lost his right to take her back without tahlil. This shows the divorce was final and irrevocable, which only occurs with three divorces (for a free woman) or three equivalent (for a slave).
The dalala (indication) is qati (definitive) in the Hanafi view.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars (not specified) opined that three divorces before consummation do not all fall, or that tahlil is not required. The commentary refutes this as mistaken.
The Hanafi position is that all three fall and tahlil is required.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that simultaneous three fall; sequential only the first. This is based on the Companions' fatwa and consistent Hanafi usul.
Authority weight: Zahir al-Riwayah.

Book of Divorce / Likening of Divorce and its Description and Kinayah of Divorce. And whoever divorces her three times before intercourse, they fall; and if he separates (the pronouncements), she becomes irrevocably divorced by the first and the second does not fall. So in: 'You are divorced one and one' one falls. This applies to a free woman. As for a slave woman, two are equivalent to three for a free woman. Commentary: 'He said: this' means the falling of three with intention of three, and the falling of one with intention of two. 'He said: three' and likewise if he divorces her two together, like his saying: 'you are divorced two', and likewise if he says: 'one and a half' two fall, because it is one sentence. And if he says: 'half and one' one falls. As in al-Bahr. 'He said: they fall' means those three divorces. Indeed, the non-consummated wife, although she becomes irrevocable with one due to having no waiting period, the irrevocability there is not severe, so it is permissible for him to marry her without tahlil. If he divorces her three together, they all fall, and she becomes such that she is not permissible for him until she marries another husband. This is the fatwa of Ibn Abbas and Abu Hurairah when they were asked about a man who divorced his wife before consummation. The questioner said: 'My divorce of her was only one.' Ibn Abbas said: 'You have released from your hand what advantage you had.' Recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta and Abu Dawud in his Sunan. Whoever thinks that three do not fall on the non-consummated wife or that tahlil is not required with the falling of three is mistaken, as Ibn al-Humam elaborated in Fath al-Qadir. 'He said: if he separates' is past tense from tafriq, and it is more general than being by sentences, like 'you are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced', or by report, like 'you are divorced, divorced, divorced', or by description, like 'you are divorced one, one, one.'
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on the effect of three divorces pronounced before consummation. The matn states: if a man pronounces three divorces before intercourse, they all take effect. However, if he separates the pronouncements (e.g., says one, then later another), the wife becomes irrevocably divorced (bain) with the first, and the second does not fall because after the first irrevocable divorce, the marriage is dissolved and there is no remaining marital bond to which the second could attach. The example given is the phrase 'you are divorced one and one' (using 'and' which implies separation) — only one divorce falls. The commentary clarifies that this ruling applies to a free woman; for a slave woman, two divorces are equivalent to three for a free woman (since a slave can only be divorced twice before becoming irrevocable). The commentator expands on the types of separation: it can be by separate sentences, by repetition of the word 'divorced', or by describing with numbers like 'one and one and one'. The proof is the hadith from Ibn Abbas and Abu Hurairah. This is a Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Key Terms
بينونة (baynunah): Irrevocable separation that dissolves the marriage, requiring a new contract if remarriage is desired.
تحليل (tahlil): The act of the woman marrying another man, consummating, and then divorcing, so that she becomes permissible to her first husband.
عدة (iddah): Waiting period after divorce; for non-consummated wife, there is no iddah (only one menstrual cycle if divorced after consummation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) One
C) Two
D) None

Key Takeaway
Simultaneous three divorces before consummation all fall and require tahlil; sequential only the first falls, allowing remarriage without tahlil.

Divorce by Number and the Effect of 'Before' and 'After'
Ruling with proof on number attachment and before/after phrasing in talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that divorce falls by the number attached to the utterance, and distinguish between the effects of 'before' and 'after' wording in divorce sentences.

Core Idea
Divorce is counted by the number attached, not by the word “divorced” alone.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah (a commentary on Al-Wiqayah) explains a critical Hanafi principle: when a husband says “you are divorced one (or three)”, the divorce takes effect according to that number, not by the word “divorced” itself. This is based on an usul rule that the beginning of a sentence can be suspended by what follows. The passage then dives into the grammatical intricacies of 'before' and 'after' in divorce phrases, showing how the position of these particles determines which divorces actually fall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
Extract the main ruling: divorce falls by the number connected to it (بعددٍ قُرِنَ بالطلاق), not by the word 'divorce' itself.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
2
Understand the usul dalil
The proof is the principle 'صدر الكلام يتوقف على ما بعده إذا كان في آخره مغير له' – the beginning of speech is suspended by what follows if it changes the meaning. Therefore, 'you are divorced' is contingent on the number.
The dalil is a qiyas khafi based on linguistic usage.
3
Analyze the 'before/after' cases
Using the Zaid and Amr examples, determine whether 'before' or 'after' describes the first or second divorce based on whether it is added to a noun or a pronoun.
Grammatical roles directly affect legal outcome.
4
Derive practical outcomes
For a non-consummated wife: 'one before one' → only first falls; 'one after it one' → only first falls; 'one after one' → both fall together.
The key is the remaining marriage capacity (محل).

ويقع بعددٍ قُرِنَ بالطلاق ، لا به ) ، فيلغو أنتِ طالق لو ماتت قبل ذِكْرِ العدد، وبانت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة أو بعدها واحدة واحدة) : لأن الواحدةً الأولى وصفت بالقبلية (۳) ، فلما وقعت لم يبق للثانية محلّ.
[١] قوله : ويقع ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه إذا لم يقرن صيغة الطلاق بالعــدد يقعُ الطلاق بها، وإن قرنها بعدد يقع الطلاق بذلك العدد لا بنفس الصيغة لما تقرر في الأصول أنّ صدر الكلام يتوقف على ما بعده إذا كان في آخره مغير له، فيكون أنت طالق واحدة أو ثلاثاً لغواً لا يقع به شيء إن ماتت قبل ذكر العدد.
[١] قوله : لم يبقَ للثانية محلّ ؛ توضيحه : أنّ الظرف مثل : «قبل» و«بعد» حيث ذكر بين شيئين إن أضيف إلى ظاهر كان صفة الأوّل مثل : جاءني زيد قبل عمرو، أو بعد عمرو ، فإنّ القبلية والبعدية هاهنا صفة لزيد.

وفي : أنت طالق واحدة بعد واحدة تقع ثنتان معاً ؛ لأنَّ البعدية هاهنا صفةٌ للواحدة الأولى الواقعة لا محالة، واقتضى ذلك وقوع واحدة قبلها، ليصح توصيف

And it falls by the number that is connected with the divorce, not by (the word divorce) itself. So 'you are divorced' becomes null if she dies before mentioning the number. And she becomes irrevocably divorced if he says 'one before one' or 'after it one one' because the first one is described by being before, so when it falls, there remains no place for the second. Commentary: The summary is that if the divorce formula is not connected with a number, the divorce falls by it; if it is connected with a number, the divorce falls by that number, not by the formula itself, due to the established principle in Usul that the beginning of speech is suspended until what comes after if there is something at the end that changes it. So 'you are divorced one' or 'three' is void and nothing falls if she dies before mentioning the number. Explanation: The adverbs like 'before' and 'after' when mentioned between two things, if added to an evident noun, are attributes of the first, e.g., 'Zaid came before Amr' or 'after Amr' – the beforeness and afterness are attributes of Zaid. If added to a pronoun referring to the first, they are meaningful attributes of the second, e.g., 'Zaid came before him Amr' or 'after him Amr' – the described with beforeness or afterness is Amr, not Zaid. Having established this, we say: if he says to a non-consummated wife, 'you are divorced one before one', the beforeness is an attribute of the first one, so the first one falls and the second does not fall due to lack of remaining place (no iddah). Similarly if he says 'you are divorced one after it one' – the afterness here is an attribute of the second one, so the first falls and the other is void. And in 'you are divorced one after one' two fall together because the afterness here is an attribute of the first one that inevitably falls, and this necessitates the occurrence of one before it for the description of the current one to be correct, and it has passed that past instantiation in the chapter of divorce works.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage explains a key rule in Hanafi divorce law: when a man pronounces divorce and attaches a number (e.g., 'you are divorced one' or 'three'), the divorce takes effect according to that number, not according to the word 'divorced' itself. This is based on the usul principle that the beginning of a sentence can be suspended by what follows if it changes the meaning. Therefore, if a man says 'you are divorced one' but the wife dies before he finishes the word 'one', no divorce occurs because the number was necessary for the divorce to count. The passage then delves into the complex issue of using the words 'before' and 'after' in divorce. Using examples from Arabic grammar (Zaid and Amr), it clarifies that when 'before' or 'after' is mentioned, its grammatical function (whether it is an adjective for the first or second noun) determines which divorce falls. For a non-consummated wife: if he says 'one before one', the first falls and the second does not, because after the first divorce the wife becomes irrevocably divorced (no iddah) and there is no remaining marriage for the second to attach to. If he says 'one after it one', the first falls and the second is void. But if he says 'one after one' (without a pronoun), two divorces fall together because 'after' here describes the first divorce (meaning it occurs after another divorce), so both occur simultaneously. This is a detailed application of grammatical analysis to fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
قُرِنَ (qurina): Connected or attached, meaning the number is joined to the divorce utterance.
صدر الكلام (sadr al-kalam): The beginning of speech. In Usul, the opening part of a sentence.
القبلية والبعدية (al-qabliyyah wa al-ba'diyyah): The qualities of being before and after, used as grammatical attributes in divorce sentences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One
C) Two
D) Three

Key Takeaway
Divorce falls by the number attached; 'before' and 'after' particles must be parsed grammatically to determine which divorces take effect.

Effect of Number on Divorce Pronouncement
Ruling on the effect of number in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that when a number is attached to the word talaq, the divorce occurs by that number, and explain the consequence if the wife dies before the number is mentioned.

Core Idea
The number determines the count; the word 'talaq' alone is suspended until the number is spoken.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes a foundational rule: if the divorce form is explicitly linked to a number (e.g., 'you are divorced three'), the divorce takes effect by that number, not by the form itself. The reasoning is derived from a usul principle: the beginning of a sentence is dependent on its end when the end modifies the meaning. Thus, if the wife dies before the number is uttered, no divorce occurs because the essential condition (the number) was not fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The text states: 'if the form of divorce is not connected to the number, the divorce occurs by it; but if connected, the divorce occurs by the number, not by the form itself.' This is the core ruling.
2
Understand the usul basis
The reason is that 'the beginning of speech is suspended upon what follows it if the end contains a modifier.' This is a principle from Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
3
Apply to a death scenario
If the husband says 'you are divorced...' and the wife dies before he mentions the number, the pronouncement is void (laghw). No divorce takes effect.
4
Contrast with unnumbered talaq
If no number is attached, one divorce occurs immediately upon utterance. The presence of a number changes the operative element.

[١] قوله : ويقع ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه إذا لم يقرن صيغة الطلاق بالعــدد يقعُ الطلاق بها، وإن قرنها بعدد يقع الطلاق بذلك العدد لا بنفس الصيغة لما تقرر في الأصول أنّ صدر الكلام يتوقف على ما بعده إذا كان في آخره مغير له، فيكون أنت طالق واحدة أو ثلاثاً لغواً لا يقع به شيء إن ماتت قبل ذكر العدد.
[١] قوله : لم يبقَ للثانية محلّ؛ توضيحه : أنّ الظرف مثل : «قبل» و«بعد» حيث ذكر بين شيئين إن أضيف إلى ظاهر كان صفة الأوّل مثل : جاءني زيد قبل عمرو، أو بعد عمرو، فإنّ القبلية والبعدية هاهنا صفة لزيد.
وإن أضيف إلى الضمير الراجع إلى الأوّل كان صفة معنوية للثاني مثل : جاءني زيد قبله عمرو، أو بعده عمرو، فإنّ الموصوف بالقبلية والبعدية هاهنا هو عمرو لا زيد. إذا تمهد هذا فنقول : إذا قال لغير الموطوءة : أنت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة، فالقبلية صفة للواحدة الأولى، فإنّها المتصفة بكونها متقدماً على الواحدة الثانية التي أضيف إليها قبل، فتقع الواحدة الأولى ولا تقع الثانية؛ لعدم بقاء المحلية؛ لعدم العدة، وكذا إذا قال : أنت طالق واحدة بعدها واحدة فإنّ البعدية هاهنا صفة للواحدة الثانية، فتقع الأولى وتلغو الأخرى.
وفي : أنت طالق واحدة بعد واحدة تقع ثنتان معاً؛ لأنَّ البعدية هاهنا صفةٌ للواحدة الأولى الواقعة لا محالة، واقتضى ذلك وقوع واحدة قبلها، ليصح توصيف الواحدة الواقعة في الحال بالبعدية، وقد مرّ أنّ الإيقاع الماضي في باب الطلاقِ إيقاع في الحال؛ لعدم إمكان الإنشاء في الزمان الماضي، فيجعل كأنه أوقعها في الحال، فيقترنان ويقعان معاً.
وبانت طالق واحدة قبلها واحدة، أو بعد واحدة، أو مع واحدة، أو معها واحدة ثنتان): أما في قبلها وبعد واحدة؛ فلأن الواحدة الأُولى، وهي التي يوقعها في الحال، وصفت بالبعدية، فاقتضت وقوع واحدة متقدمة عليها، لكن لا قدرة له على الإيقاع في الزمان الماضي، فيقع في الحال، فتكون الواحدةُ الأُولى والثانية متقارنتين، أي في الوجود وكلـه لـقـيـام المحلية بعد وقوع الأول، وأما في مـع ومعها فظاهر".

[٢] قوله: فظاهر؛ فإنّ كلمة «مع» موضوعةٌ للقران والمعية الزمانية، فتدل على اقتران ما قبلها وما بعدها. فإن قلت: قد يجيء للتأخير أيضاً، كما في قوله: إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا (١).
(١) الشرح: ٦.
And it occurs by a number that is connected to the divorce, not by it, so it is null: 'You are divorced' if she dies before mentioning the number, and she becomes separated by one divorce before one, or after it one by one. [1] His statement: 'And it occurs...' — its summary: that if the form of divorce is not connected to the number, the divorce occurs by it; but if it is connected to a number, the divorce occurs by that number, not by the form itself, because it is established in usul that the beginning of speech is suspended upon what follows it if the end contains a modifier for it. So 'You are divorced one or three' is null and nothing occurs by it if she dies before mentioning the number. [1] His statement: 'There remains no place for the second' — its clarification: that the adverb like 'before' and 'after' when mentioned between two things: if it is added to a manifest noun, it is an attribute of the first, like 'Zaid came to me before Amr' or 'after Amr', for the 'before-ness' and 'after-ness' here are attributes of Zaid. And if it is added to a pronoun referring back to the first, it is a semantic attribute of the second, like 'Zaid came to me before him Amr' or 'after him Amr', for the thing described with 'before-ness' and 'after-ness' here is Amr, not Zaid. Once this is settled, we say: if he says to a non-consummated woman: 'You are divorced one before one', then the 'before-ness' is an attribute of the first one, for it is described as being prior to the second one to which 'before' is added, so the first one falls and the second does not fall, due to lack of remaining legal capacity due to lack of 'idda. Similarly, if he says: 'You are divorced one after it one', then the 'after-ness' here is an attribute of the second one, so the first falls and the other is null. And in: 'You are divorced one after one', two [divorces] occur together, because the 'after-ness' here is an attribute of the first one that inevitably falls, and that necessitates the occurrence of one before it to make valid the description of the one occurring now with 'after-ness'. And it has passed that a past effective in the chapter of divorce is an effective in the present due to impossibility of creating in the past time, so it is treated as if he effected it now, so they become concurrent and occur together. And she becomes divorced by one before it one, or after one, or with one, or with her one, two. As for 'before it one' and 'after one', because the first one, which he effects now, is described with 'after-ness', necessitating the occurrence of one preceding it, but he has no power to effect in the past time, so it occurs now, thus the first and second become concurrent, i.e., in existence. And this is due to the existence of legal capacity after the occurrence of the first. And as for 'with' and 'with her', it is apparent. (1) i.e., not by the divorce, because the beginning of speech is suspended upon mention of the number, so it does not convey the ruling before it. See: Durar al-Hukkam 1:366. (2) He means explicitly, because the 'after-ness' in his statement 'after it one' is an attribute of the last, so the first occurs before it necessarily. See: al-Shurunbulaliyya 1:367. [11] His statement: 'described with after-ness' — as for 'one after one', the description of the first one with after-ness is apparent. And as for 'before it one', it is because we explained that the 'before-ness' there is an attribute of the second in meaning, even if not in wording, and the second being before the first entails that the first is after the second, because 'before-ness' and 'after-ness' are correlative matters, one's existence and conception necessitates the other's existence and conception. [2] His statement: 'it is apparent' — because the word 'with' is placed for concurrency and temporal simultaneity, so it indicates the conjunction of what precedes it and what follows it. If you say: it may come for delay as well, as in His saying: 'Indeed, with hardship is ease.' (1) Explanation: 6.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling of divorce when a number is attached to the word 'talaq' and when temporal adverbs like 'before', 'after', and 'with' are used. The passage type is a ruling with linguistic analysis (talil).

The first part establishes a general principle: if the form of divorce (e.g., 'you are divorced') is not linked to a number, the divorce itself occurs immediately. But if it is linked to a number like 'one' or 'three', the divorce occurs by that number, not by the bare word 'talaq'. This is due to the usul principle that the beginning of a sentence is suspended upon its end if the end changes the meaning. Thus, if a husband says 'you are divorced one' and then his wife dies before he mentions the number, the divorce is void because the number was an integral part of the conditional. This is a Hanafi position.

The second part delves into the linguistic nuances of adverbs 'before', 'after', and 'with' when used in divorce pronouncements. The commentator explains that when an adverb like 'qabl' is added to a manifest noun, it describes the first noun. But when added to a pronoun referring back to the first, it describes the second. This grammatical rule determines how many divorces occur.

Key scenarios:
- 'You are divorced one before one' (li-ghayr al-mawtu'a): The first divorce falls, the second is null because after one divorce the woman becomes non-mahall (no longer eligible for more divorces) in the case of a non-consummated marriage.
- 'You are divorced one after it one' (bada-ha wahidah): The first falls, the second is null.
- 'You are divorced one after one' (ba'da wahidah): Two divorces occur together because the 'after-ness' describes the first, implying a prior divorce, but since past enactment is impossible, both occur simultaneously.
- 'You are divorced one before it one' (qablaha wahidah): Two divorces occur together because 'before-ness' describes the second, implying a prior divorce, leading to concurrence.
- With the word 'with' (ma'a), the natural meaning is conjunction, so both fall together.

The text also notes that for a consummated woman, in the first two scenarios, two divorces also occur because the 'idda period provides a legal capacity for the second divorce.

This entire discussion is purely within the Hanafi madhhab and relies on Arabic grammar as the proof mechanism.

Key Terms
عدد (adad): The number explicitly attached to the word talaq (e.g., one, two, three).
لغو (laghw): Null, void; referring to a pronouncement that has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a man says 'You are divorced' and his wife dies before he says 'three'?
A) Three divorces occur.
B) One divorce occurs.
C) No divorce occurs.
D) The pronouncement is postponed until the wife's estate is settled.

Key Takeaway
A divorce pronouncement with an attached number is effective by the number, and if the wife dies before the number is spoken, no divorce occurs.

Effect of Temporal Adverbs (Qabl, Ba'd, Ma'a) in Divorce
Linguistic analysis of adverbs in divorce pronouncements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of divorces that occur when a husband uses 'before' (qabl), 'after' (ba'd), or 'with' (ma'a) in his pronouncement, based on the grammatical attachment of the adverb.

Core Idea
The grammatical attachment of the adverb decides which divorce is described, determining the count in complex pronouncements.

This passage delves into the linguistic analysis of temporal adverbs in divorce. When a husband says 'you are divorced one before one' or 'one after one', the classification of the adverb (whether it is an attribute of the first or second divorce) determines the count. The Hanafi school uses Arabic grammar as a precise tool to derive these rulings, leading to different outcomes for consummated and non-consummated marriages.

Guided Path
1
Learn the grammatical rule for adverbs
When an adverb like 'qabl' is added to a manifest noun, it describes the first noun. When added to a pronoun referring to the first, it describes the second.
Example: 'Zaid came before Amr' (manifest noun) -> 'before-ness' describes Zaid. 'Zaid came before him, Amr' (pronoun) -> describes Amr.
2
Apply to 'one before one' (qabl)
In 'one before one', 'before' is added to 'one' (manifest), so it describes the first divorce. Thus the first falls immediately, and the second does not fall because after one divorce, the wife becomes non-mahall (non-consummated).
3
Apply to 'one after one' (ba'd) – two cases
Case 1: 'one after one' (ba'da wahidah) – 'after' added to manifest noun -> describes first -> first falls, second null. Case 2: 'one after it one' (ba'daha wahidah) – 'after' added to pronoun -> describes second -> first falls, second null (same result due to lack of mahall). But if the wife is consummated, two divorces occur because the 'idda provides capacity.
The text also discusses 'one after one' (without pronoun) where the first is described with 'after-ness', necessitating a prior divorce; since past enactment is impossible, both occur now.
4
Apply to 'with' (ma'a)
The word 'with' indicates concurrency, so both divorces occur together simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقع بعددٍ قُرِنَ بالطلاق، لا به ، فيلغو أنتِ طالق لو ماتت قبل ذكر العدد، وبانت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة، أو بعدها واحدة واحدة
[١] قوله : ويقع ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه إذا لم يقرن صيغة الطلاق بالعــدد يقعُ الطلاق بها، وإن قرنها بعدد يقع الطلاق بذلك العدد لا بنفس الصيغة لما تقرر في الأصول أنّ صدر الكلام يتوقف على ما بعده إذا كان في آخره مغير له، فيكون أنت طالق واحدة أو ثلاثاً لغواً لا يقع به شيء إن ماتت قبل ذكر العدد.
[١] قوله : لم يبقَ للثانية محلّ؛ توضيحه : أنّ الظرف مثل : «قبل» و«بعد» حيث ذكر بين شيئين إن أضيف إلى ظاهر كان صفة الأوّل مثل : جاءني زيد قبل عمرو، أو بعد عمرو، فإنّ القبلية والبعدية هاهنا صفة لزيد.
وإن أضيف إلى الضمير الراجع إلى الأوّل كان صفة معنوية للثاني مثل : جاءني زيد قبله عمرو، أو بعده عمرو، فإنّ الموصوف بالقبلية والبعدية هاهنا هو عمرو لا زيد. إذا تمهد هذا فنقول : إذا قال لغير الموطوءة : أنت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة، فالقبلية صفة للواحدة الأولى، فإنّها المتصفة بكونها متقدماً على الواحدة الثانية التي أضيف إليها قبل، فتقع الواحدة الأولى ولا تقع الثانية؛ لعدم بقاء المحلية؛ لعدم العدة، وكذا إذا قال : أنت طالق واحدة بعدها واحدة فإنّ البعدية هاهنا صفة للواحدة الثانية، فتقع الأولى وتلغو الأخرى.
وفي : أنت طالق واحدة بعد واحدة تقع ثنتان معاً؛ لأنَّ البعدية هاهنا صفةٌ للواحدة الأولى الواقعة لا محالة، واقتضى ذلك وقوع واحدة قبلها، ليصح توصيف الواحدة الواقعة في الحال بالبعدية، وقد مرّ أنّ الإيقاع الماضي في باب الطلاقِ إيقاع في الحال؛ لعدم إمكان الإنشاء في الزمان الماضي، فيجعل كأنه أوقعها في الحال، فيقترنان ويقعان معاً.
وبانت طالق واحدة قبلها واحدة، أو بعد واحدة، أو مع واحدة، أو معها واحدة ثنتان): أما في قبلها وبعد واحدة؛ فلأن الواحدة الأُولى، وهي التي يوقعها في الحال، وصفت بالبعدية، فاقتضت وقوع واحدة متقدمة عليها، لكن لا قدرة له على الإيقاع في الزمان الماضي، فيقع في الحال، فتكون الواحدةُ الأُولى والثانية متقارنتين، أي في الوجود وكلـه لـقـيـام المحلية بعد وقوع الأول، وأما في مـع ومعها فظاهر".
(۱) أي لا بالطلاق؛ لأن صدر الكلام موقوف على ذكر العدد فلا يفيد الحكم قبله. ينظر: درر الحكام (١: ٣٦٦).
(٢) يعني بالصراحة؛ لأن البعدية في قوله: بعدها واحدة صفة الأخيرة فوقع الأولى قبلها ضرورة. ينظر: «الشرنبلالية» (١: ٣٦٧).
[١١] قوله: وصفت بالبعدية؛ أما في قوله: واحدة بعد واحدة فتوصيف الواحدة الأولى بالبعدية ظاهر، وأما في قبلها واحدة فلما بيّنا أنّ القبلية هناك وصف للواحدة الثانية معنى، وإن لم يكن لفظاً، وكون الواحدة الثانية قبل الواحدة الأولى مستلزم لكون الواحدة الأولى بعد الثانية، فإنّ القبليّة والبعدية من الأمور المتضايفة، يستلزم وجود أحدهما وتعقله وجود الآخر وتعقله.

Commentary
This passage from Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling of divorce when a number is attached to the word 'talaq' and when temporal adverbs like 'before', 'after', and 'with' are used. The passage type is a ruling with linguistic analysis (talil).

Thus, if a husband says 'you are divorced one' and then his wife dies before he mentions the number, the divorce is void because the number was an integral part of the conditional. This is a Hanafi position.

- 'You are divorced one after one' (ba'da wahidah): Two divorces occur together because the 'after-ness' describes the first, implying a prior divorce, but since past enactment is impossible, both occur simultaneously.

Key Terms
قبل (qabl): Temporal adverb meaning 'before'. When attached to a manifest noun, describes the first; when to a pronoun, describes the second.
بعد (ba'd): Temporal adverb meaning 'after'. Same grammatical rule as 'qabl'.
مع (ma'a): Preposition meaning 'with', indicating simultaneity. In talaq, it causes both divorces to occur concurrently.

Talaq — Divorce

Conditional Divorce with Multiple Pronouncements: Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Explaining the intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce with multiple talaqs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on how many divorces occur when a condition is presented with multiple talaq pronouncements, and explain the reasoning behind the difference.

Core Idea
The dispute is not about the meaning of 'wa' but about sequential vs simultaneous attachment of multiple conditional consequences.

In Hanafi fiqh, conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) is valid. A disagreement arose between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), over what happens when a man says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one.' Does one divorce fall or two? The answer depends on how the multiple pronouncements are attached to the condition: sequentially or all at once. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Start with the standard case: a husband says to his wife (who is not a non-virgin, i.e., she has not been divorced before) 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one.' This is a conditional with multiple pronouncements. Key variable: whether the condition is presented (stated first) or delayed.
The text also discusses the non-virgin (al-mawtu'a) separately.
2
State the positions
For the presented condition: Abu Hanifa holds that one divorce occurs; the Sahibayn hold that two divorces occur. For the delayed condition, both agree two divorces occur.
3
Understand the reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that when the condition is presented, the first divorce is directly attached to the condition, and the second is attached through the first in a sequential manner. When the condition occurs, only the first takes effect because the second has no place (mahall) after the first has separated the wife. The Sahibayn argue that all pronouncements are attached simultaneously, so all fall at once.
The reasoning is based on the structure of the sentence: presented condition makes the first part complete and independent; delayed condition makes everything interdependent.
4
Recognize the common misunderstanding
The dispute is not about the linguistic meaning of the conjunction 'wa' (which indicates absolute conjunction for all). Rather, it is about the manner of attachment of the consequences to the condition.
This is clarified in the commentary.
5
Note the mufta bihi
Later scholars like Ibn al-Humam preferred the view of the Sahibayn, so the operative ruling for fatwa is that when the condition is presented with multiple pronouncements, all divorces occur at once.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الموطوءة ثنتان في كلّها ، وبأنت طالق واحدة وواحدة إن دخلت الدار ثنتان لو دخلت ، وواحدة إن قدّم شرطه (وفي الموطوءة ثنتان في كلّها. وبأنت طالق واحدة وواحدة إن دخلت الدار ثنتان" لو دخلت، وواحدة إن قدّم شرطه) : أي قال : إن دخلتِ الدَّار فأنتِ طالق واحدة وواحدة، فعند تقدم الشرط تقعُ واحدة، وهذا في غير الموطوءة ؛ فإنّ الواحدة الثانية تعلقت بالشَّرطِ بواسطة الأولى قلت : هب ، ولكن لا بد من الحمل عليه من قرنية عقليّة أو لفظية ؛ وإذ ليس فليس [١] قوله : وواحدة ؛ العطف بالفاء في هذا البحث كالعطف بالواو، فتقع واحدةٌ إن قدّم الشرط اتفاقاً على الأصح ، وتلغو الثانية وثنتان إن أخره، وفي العطف بثُمَ إن أخره تنجزت واحدة ولغا ما بعدها، ولو موطوءة تعلّق الأخير وتنجز ما قبله، وإن قدّم الشرط لغـا الثالث وتنجز الثاني وتعلّق الأوّل فيقع عند الشرط بعد التزوج الثاني، ولو موطوءة تعلّق الأول وتنجز ما بعده، وعندهما تعلّق الكلّ بالشرط قدمه أو أخره. كذا في «البحر». [٢]قوله : ثنتان ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه لو أخَّرَ الشرط وقال : أنت طالق واحدة وواحدة ، أو : أنت طالق وطالق إن دخلت الدار، يقع ثنتان سواء كانت مدخولاً بها أو لا، وإن كرّر لفظ الواحدة أو الطلاق ثلاثاً يقعُ الثلاث معاً فيهما ، وإن قدّم الشرط بأن قال : إن دخلت لدار فأنت طالق واحدة وواحدة ، أو : فأنت طالق وطالق، تقع واحدة عنده في غير الموطوءة، وثنتان عندهما ، وقس عليه التكرير ثلاثاً. والوجه في ذلك على ما فصله الشارح في «التوضيح» و«التنقيح»، والتفتازاني في «التلويح) وغيرهما : إنّ تعليق الأجزية بالشرط عنده على سبيل الترتيب والتعاقب ، فإن قوله : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ، جملة كاملة مستغنية عمّا بعدها، فيحصل به التعليق بالشرط ، وقوله : وطالق ، جملة ناقصة مفتقرة في الإفادة إلى الأولى، فيكون فإذا وُجِدَ الشَّرط يقعُ بهذا الترتيب ، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وأما عندهما يقعُ ثنتان، وتحقيقه في أصول الفقه في حروف المعاني . تعليق الثانية بعد تعليق الأولى، وإذا كان التعليق مترتباً كان الوقوع أيضاً كذلك ؛ لأنّ المعلق كالمنجز عند وجود الشرط، وفي المنجز تبين غير الموطوءة الأولى، ولا تصادف الثانية محلاً، وكذا الثالثة. فكذا في المعلق تبين بالواحدة عند دخول الدار، بخلاف ما إذا قَدَّمَ الجزاء، فإنّ الكل متعلّق بالشرط دفعة ؛ لأنه إذا كان في آخر الكلام ما يغيّر أوله يتوقف الأول على الآخر، فلا يكون فيه ترتيب في التعليق ولا في الوقوع ، وعندهما: الكل متعلّق بالشرط دفعة قدم أو أخر، فيقع الكل دفعة. [١]قوله : بهذا الترتيب ؛ ظنَّ بعضُهم من هذه المسألة الخلافية أنّ الواو عند أبي حنيفة الله للترتيب، وللمقارنة عندهما وهو ليس بصحيح، بل الواو عند الكل لمطلق الجمع، من دون ملاحظة للمقارنة والترتيب، ومنشأ الخلاف هو تعلّق الأجزية بالشرط مرتباً عنده ، فتنزل كذلك، وعندهما تنزل دفعة، وتفصيله في «التنقيح» و «التوضيح في «بحث حرف الواو عند البحث عن «الحقيقية والمجاز». [٢] قوله : وأما عندهما ... الخ ؛ رجّح قولهما ابن الهمام في «الفتح»، وأشير إليه في «الميزان» و«الأسرار» و«التنقيح» و«البحر». [٣] قوله : في حروف المعاني ؛ قد جرت عادة الأصوليين بالبحث عـن بعـض الحروف، كحروف الجرّ والعطف وغيرها، وعن الظروفِ عقيب بحث الحقيقة والمجاز ؛ لاشتداد الحاجة إليها من جهة توقف كثير من المسائل عليها، ويسمّى ذلك البحث يبحث حروف المعاني. وفي إطلاق الحروف على الظروف، مع أنها أسماء تغليب، أو المراد بالحروف مطلق الكلمات، وفي الإضافة إلى المعاني احتراز عن حروف المباني، وهي حروف الهجاء التي تتركب منها الكلمات، وليس لها في أنفسها معنى.
And in the non-virgin [wife], two [divorces] in all of it. And 'You are divorced one and one if you enter the house' [results in] two if she enters, and one if he presents the condition. (And in the non-virgin, two in all of it. And 'You are divorced one and one if you enter the house' [results in] two if she enters, and one if he presents the condition): meaning he said: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced one and one.' So when the condition is presented, one occurs, and this is in the non-virgin; for the second one is attached to the condition by means of the first. I said: Yes, but it must be carried upon a rational or verbal indication; and if not, then not. His statement 'and one': the conjunction with 'fa' in this discussion is like the conjunction with 'wa', so one occurs if the condition is presented, unanimously according to the most correct, and the second is null, and two if he delays it. And with the conjunction 'thumma' if he delays it, one is executed and what follows is null. And if [the wife] is non-virgin, the last is attached and what precedes it is executed. And if he presents the condition, the third is null, the second is executed, and the first is attached, so it occurs upon the condition after the second marriage. And if [the wife] is non-virgin, the first is attached and what follows is executed. And according to the two [Sahibayn], all are attached to the condition whether he presents or delays it. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. His statement 'two ... etc.': its summary: that if he delays the condition and says 'You are divorced one and one' or 'You are divorced and divorced if you enter the house', two occur whether she is consummated or not. And if he repeats the word 'one' or 'divorce' three times, all three occur together in both cases. And if he presents the condition and says 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced one and one' or 'then you are divorced and divorced', one occurs according to him [Abu Hanifa] in the non-virgin, and two according to the two [Sahibayn]. And analogize upon that the repetition of three. And the explanation for that, as elaborated by the commentator in 'al-Tawdih' and 'al-Tanqih', and al-Taftazani in 'al-Talwih' and others: that the attachment of the consequences to the condition according to him is on the basis of order and succession. For his statement 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced' is a complete sentence independent of what follows it, so attachment to the condition is established by it. And his statement 'and divorced' is an incomplete sentence needing the first for completion. So when the condition is found, it occurs in this order. And this is according to Abu Hanifa. As for according to the two, two occur. And its verification is in Usul al-Fiqh in the section on particles of meanings. The attachment of the second after the attachment of the first. And if the attachment is ordered, then the occurrence is also ordered; because the conditional is like the executed upon the existence of the condition. And in the executed, the non-virgin becomes separated by the first, and the second finds no place, and similarly the third. So likewise in the conditional, she becomes separated by one upon entering the house, contrary to when he presents the condition, for then all are attached to the condition at once; because if there is something in the end of the speech that changes its beginning, the first depends on the last, so there is no order in attachment nor in occurrence. And according to the two: all are attached to the condition at once whether he presents or delays, so all occur at once. [The commentator said]: 'His statement: in this order.' Some thought from this controversial issue that 'wa' according to Abu Hanifa indicates order, and according to the two indicates simultaneity, but this is not correct. Rather 'wa' for all indicates absolute conjunction without consideration of simultaneity or order. The origin of the difference is the attachment of consequences to the condition in an ordered manner according to him, so they descend likewise. And according to the two, they descend at once. Its detail is in 'al-Tanqih' and 'al-Tawdih' in the discussion of the letter 'wa' in the section on 'al-Haqiqah wa al-Majaz'. [The commentator said]: 'His statement: And as for according to the two ... etc.' Ibn al-Humam in 'al-Fath' preferred their view, and it is indicated in 'al-Mizan', 'al-Asrar', 'al-Tanqih', and 'al-Bahr'. [The commentator said]: 'His statement: in the section on particles of meanings.' It is the custom of Usul scholars to discuss some particles, such as prepositions and conjunctions and others, and about adverbs after the discussion of 'Haqiqah and Majaz', due to great need for them because many issues depend on them. That discussion is called the discussion of particles of meanings. And the application of 'particles' to adverbs, even though they are nouns, is by way of predominance, or by 'particles' meaning absolute words. And the addition to 'meanings' is to distinguish from letters of construct (huruf al-mabani), which are the letters of the alphabet from which words are composed and which have no meaning in themselves.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) when multiple pronouncements are made. The core issue is how many divorces occur when a condition is phrased with conjunctions like 'wa' (and), 'fa' (then), or 'thumma' (then). The text explains the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

First, the matn states that for a non-virgin (al-mawtu'a) two divorces occur in all cases. Then it discusses the phrase 'You are divorced one and one if you enter the house': if the condition is presented (i.e., the condition is stated first), one divorce occurs according to Abu Hanifa; if the condition is delayed (the condition is stated after the divorces), two divorces occur. This applies when the wife is not a non-virgin (i.e., when she is still in her first marriage and not yet divorced). For a non-virgin, the rules differ because she has already been divorced once.

The commentary clarifies that the conjunction 'fa' is equivalent to 'wa' in this ruling. If the condition is presented, one divorce occurs by agreement (on the most correct view), and the second is cancelled. If the condition is delayed, two divorces occur. With 'thumma', if the condition is delayed, one divorce is executed and the rest are void. For a non-virgin, if the condition is delayed, the last divorce is attached and the previous ones are executed; if the condition is presented, the third is void, the second is executed, and the first is attached (occurs upon the condition after remarriage). According to the Sahibayn, all divorces are attached to the condition regardless of order.

The passage then details the reasoning: Abu Hanifa holds that when the condition is stated first, the first divorce is directly attached to the condition, and subsequent divorces are attached through the first in a sequential manner. Thus, only one divorce occurs because the second has no place after the first has already separated the wife (especially in a non-virgin). When the condition is delayed, all divorces are attached simultaneously because the beginning of the sentence is modified by the end. The Sahibayn hold that all divorces are attached at once regardless of order.

The text also addresses a common misunderstanding: the dispute is not about whether 'wa' implies order or simultaneity (it implies absolute conjunction for all), but rather about how multiple conditional consequences are attached to a single condition—sequentially according to Abu Hanifa, simultaneously according to the Sahibayn.

Finally, it notes that Ibn al-Humam in 'Fath al-Qadir' preferred the view of the Sahibayn, which is also indicated in other works. The passage ends with a note about the genre of 'Harf al-Ma'ani' in Usul al-Fiqh.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): The act of making a divorce conditional upon the occurrence of a future event.
حروف المعاني (huruf al-ma'ani): Particles that convey meanings, including prepositions, conjunctions, and adverbs, studied in Usul al-Fiqh to understand legal texts.
الموطوءة (al-mawtu'a): Literally 'the one who has been consummated' - refers to a woman who has already been divorced and then remarried and consummated the new marriage.
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic letter 'waw' used as a conjunction meaning 'and'. In this dispute, it does not imply order or simultaneity by itself; the difference arises from how multiple conditional consequences attach to the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a man says to his wife (not a non-virgin) 'If you enter the house, you are divorced one and one' (condition presented), how many divorces occur according to Abu Hanifa?
A) One divorce
B) Two divorces
C) Three divorces
D) It depends on the wife's intention

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce with multiple pronouncements hinges on whether the consequences attach sequentially or simultaneously; the majority position follows the Sahibayn, resulting in two divorces when the condition is presented.

Sequential or Simultaneous? Conditional Divorces Linked by 'Waw'
Intra-Hanafi dispute about sequence in conditional divorces linked by 'waw'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's rulings on the sequence of occurrence in conditional divorces linked by 'waw', and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each position.

Core Idea
Conditional divorces with 'waw': Abu Hanifa rules sequentially, Sahibayn rule simultaneously – the dispute is about attachment order, not the meaning of 'waw'.

When a husband suspends divorces on a condition using the conjunction 'waw' (and), does the occurrence follow the order of mention or all at once? This seemingly semantic question has a major practical impact: under Abu Hanifa, only the first wife is divorced; under the Sahibayn, all three are divorced. The key is understanding how the suspended divorce ('ilaq) relates to the condition – not the particle itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The case: a man says to his three wives, 'If you enter the house, F is divorced, and G is divorced, and H is divorced.' Where does the dispute lie?
Note the use of 'waw' between each clause.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that the divorces occur in the order mentioned. His proof: the suspended (mulaq) is like the immediate (munajjaz). If he had said 'F is divorced, G is divorced, H is divorced' without a condition, the first would take effect and the rest would be meaningless because the marriage would already be dissolved. Similarly, when the condition is met, the first divorce occurs, removing the subject for the second and third.
Thus only the first wife is divorced.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that all three divorces occur simultaneously upon the condition. Their reasoning: all the suspended consequences are attached to the condition at once, regardless of order. The 'waw' does not indicate sequence; it only conjoins. So when the condition is fulfilled, all three take effect.
Thus all three wives are divorced.
4
Clarify the common misunderstanding
The commentator explicitly states that the dispute is NOT about the meaning of 'waw'. All agree that 'waw' indicates absolute conjunction (mujarrad al-jam') without sequence or simultaneity. The real difference is about how the suspended consequences attach to the condition: Abu Hanifa sees sequential attachment because the second suspension is built on the first; the Sahibayn see simultaneous attachment.
This refines the usul analysis.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the tarjih
Practical outcome: Under Abu Hanifa, only the first wife is divorced; under the Sahibayn, all three are divorced simultaneously. Later Hanafi authority (Ibn al-Humam, al-Fath, etc.) preferred the Sahibayn's view, which is the mufta bihi position.
The fatwa today follows the Sahibayn.

Source Text

كتاب الطلاق / تشبيه الطلاق ووصفه وكنايات الطلاق
فإذا وُجِدَ الشَّرط يقعُ بهذا الترتيب، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وأما عندهما: يقعُ ثنتان، وتحقيقه في أصول الفقه في حروف المعاني .
تعليق الثانية بعد تعليق الأولى، وإذا كان التعليق مترتباً كان الوقوع أيضاً كذلك؛ لأنّ المعلق كالمنجز عند وجود الشرط، وفي المنجز تبين غير الموطوءة الأولى، ولا تصادف الثانية محلاً، وكذا الثالثة. فكذا في المعلق تبين بالواحدة عند دخول الدار، بخلاف ما إذا قَدَّمَ الجزاء، فإنّ الكل متعلّق بالشرط دفعة؛ لأنه إذا كان في آخر الكلام ما يغيّر أوله يتوقف الأول على الآخر، فلا يكون فيه ترتيب في التعليق ولا في الوقوع، وعندهما: الكل متعلّق بالشرط دفعة قدم أو أخر، فيقع الكل دفعة.

[٣] قوله : في حروف المعاني؛ قد جرت عادة الأصوليين بالبحث عـن بعـض الحروف، كحروف الجرّ والعطف وغيرها، وعن الظروفِ عقيب بحث الحقيقة والمجاز؛ لاشتداد الحاجة إليها من جهة توقف كثير من المسائل عليها، ويسمّى ذلك البحث يبحث حروف المعاني. وفي إطلاق الحروف على الظروف، مع أنها أسماء تغليب، أو المراد بالحروف مطلق الكلمات، وفي الإضافة إلى المعاني احتراز عن حروف المباني، وهي حروف الهجاء التي تتركب منها الكلمات، وليس لها في أنفسها معنى.
Book of Divorce / Likening divorce, its description, and its kinayah. When the condition is found, it occurs in this order. This according to Abu Hanifa. As for according to them (the Sahibayn): two occur. Its verification is in Usul al-Fiqh under the section on particles of meanings. The suspension of the second after the suspension of the first. Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the order of occurrence when multiple divorces are suspended on a condition linked by 'waw' (and). The issue arises in a case where a man says to his three wives, 'If you enter the house, so-and-so is divorced, and so-and-so is divorced, and so-and-so is divorced.' According to Abu Hanifa, when the condition occurs, the divorces take effect in the order mentioned: the first wife becomes irrevocably separated (ba'in), and the subsequent wives are not affected because the subject (the marriage) of the second and third has already been removed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that all three divorces occur simultaneously, regardless of the order of mention. The commentator clarifies that the difference is not about the meaning of the conjunction 'waw'—which all agree indicates absolute conjunction without specifying sequence or simultaneity—but rather about how the suspended effects relate to the condition. Abu Hanifa views the suspension as sequential because the second suspension is attached after the first, so they occur in order. The Sahibayn view all as attached to the condition at once, hence simultaneous. The commentator also notes that later scholars like Ibn al-Humam preferred the Sahibayn's view. The footnote further explains that the term 'huruf al-ma'ani' refers to grammatical particles that carry meaning, as opposed to 'huruf al-mabani' (letters of the alphabet).

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a legal effect (like divorce) upon the fulfillment of a condition.
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic conjunction 'and', used for absolute conjunction without indicating sequence or simultaneity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position of the later Hanafi school, what happens when a man says to his three wives, 'If you enter the house, F is divorced, and G is divorced, and H is divorced' and the condition is fulfilled?
A) Only F is divorced.
B) F and G are divorced, but H is not.
C) All three are divorced simultaneously.
D) None are divorced because the condition was ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorces linked by 'waw' hinges on whether the suspended consequences attach sequentially or simultaneously, not on the meaning of the particle 'waw' itself.

Definition and Classification of Kinayah in Divorce
Definition of kinayah and its classification relative to sarih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah in divorce according to the Hanafi school, distinguish it from sarih, and explain why words like 'ba'in' effect a ba'in divorce upon intention.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention; they are kinayah metaphorically, not literally, and effect ba'in divorce.

This passage clarifies the Hanafi definition of kinayah in divorce. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining when a divorce actually occurs. We will explore the usuli definition, the juristic adaptation, and the rationale behind the rule.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish sarih and kinayah in usul al-fiqh
Identify that sarih is clear in meaning (like 'anti taliq'), while kinayah is concealed (requires interpretation). Note the criteria: appearance vs. concealment in the word itself, not due to external factors.
Usul definition: haqiqah not abandoned is sarih; abandoned haqiqah or non-dominant majaz is kinayah.
2
Identify the Hanafi juristic definition of kinayah in divorce
Fuqaha' call words by which ba'in or raj'i occurs with intention, and not without, as kinayat. Examples: 'ba'in', 'battah'.
Juristic definition is broader than usul; it includes words that are not inherently concealed but are ambiguous in their legal effect.
3
Understand the objection and the answer
Objection: If the concealed meaning is divorce, why doesn't the word effect raj'i? Answer: The term 'kinayah' is applied metaphorically because the concealment is in the locus (mahall), not the word itself. Thus, the word effects ba'in divorce directly by its own meaning.
Key: The word 'ba'in' means separation; the ambiguity is which connection is severed. Marriage is implied by context, but intention specifies it.
4
Apply the rule: intention is required
Without niyyah, no divorce occurs. With niyyah, the divorce is ba'in (irrevocable).
This is the operative rule for fatwa.
