Classification of Kinayah Divorce Phrases
Correction and expansion of kinayah divorce classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kinayah divorce phrases into revocable (raj'i) and irrevocable (bain) types and explain the role of intention (niyya) in effecting divorce.

Core Idea
Not all kinayah yield bain; many yield raj'i when intended, contrary to a narrow reading.

In this passage, the commentator corrects a common misconception that only three kinayah phrases yield revocable divorce. Citing authoritative later works like al-Fath and al-Bahr, he provides an expanded list of phrases that yield raj'i divorce when intended. He then lists the phrases that yield bain divorce, explaining their linguistic roots. Understanding this classification is crucial for determining the legal consequences of a husband's choice of words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The base text stated that only three previously mentioned kinayah words yield revocable divorce; all others yield irrevocable. This is the starting point that the commentator will correct.
The three words were likely mentioned earlier in the original text.
2
Recognize the correction
The commentator says 'it is not so' and cites al-Fath, al-Bahr, and others to expand the list of raj'i phrases.
3
Extract the raj'i phrases
From the passage, list the phrases that yield revocable divorce when intended. Examples: 'Khallaytu sabila talaqik', 'Anti mutallakatun', 'Anti aṭlaqu min imra'ati fulān', etc.
Some of these phrases have internal disagreement on the necessity of intention.
4
Extract the bain phrases
From the passage, list the phrases that yield irrevocable divorce when intended. These include: 'Anti bain', 'Al-battah', 'Al-batlah', 'Al-khaliyyah', 'Al-barriyyah', 'Haram', 'Hablu 'ala gharibik', 'Ilhaqi bi-ahlik', 'Wahabtuki li-ahlik', 'Sarrahtuki', 'Faraqtuki', 'Amruki bi-yadiki', 'Anti hurrah', 'Taqanna'i', 'Takhammari', 'Istitri', 'Aghribi', 'Ukhruji', 'Idhhabi', 'Qumi', 'Ibtaghi al-azwaj'.
These words have connotations beyond divorce, so intention is required.
5
Understand the role of intention
Kinayah phrases require intention (niyya) to effect divorce. Without intention, no divorce occurs. The passage notes disagreement on whether some of the raj'i phrases are explicit or require intention.
This principle distinguishes kinayah from ṣarīḥ (explicit) phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : وبباقيها ; متعلّق بقوله : بعد سرد الألفاظ تقع واحدة بائنة، وظاهره أن الذي يقع به الرجعي من ألفاظ الكنايات منحصر في الألفاظ الثلاثة السابقة، وبباقيها تقع بائنة، وليس كذلك. فقد ذكر في «الفتح» و«البحر») وغيرهما ألفاظاً أخر من الكنايات أيضاً، تقع بها الرجعي إذا نوى، كقوله : خلّيت سبيل طلاقك، وأنت مطلقة بسكون الطاء، وأنت أطلق من امرأة فلان وهي مطلقة، وأنت طالق ، والطلاق عليك، ووهبتك طلاقك، وبعتك طلاقك ، إذا قالت : اشتريت بلا ،بدل وخذي طلاقك، وقد شاء الله طلاقك، والطلاق لك، وأنت طال، ولست لي بامرأة ، وما أنا لك بزوج، وأعرتك طلاقك، وأقرضتك طلاقتك، وطلقك الله ، وفي بعض هذه الألفاظ خلاف في التوقف على النية مبسوط في «ردّ المحتار (۳)، وغيره. [1] قوله : كانت بائن ... الخ ; هذه الألفاظ كلها تدلّ على معنى زائد على نفس الطلاق ويحتمله وغيره : فأنت بائن : من البينونة. والبتة : - بفتح الباء الوحدة وفتح التاء المثناة الفوقية المشدّدة. والبتلة : - بفتح الباء الموحدة على وزنه ، ومعنى كل منهما : القطع والتفريق. والخلية : - بفتح الخاء المعجمة ، وكسر اللام، وتشديد الياء المثناة التحتية، والبرية : على وزنه ، وهما من الخلوّ والبراءة إلي من النكاح أو غيره. ولفظ : حرام ; يحتمل حرمتها عليه ويحتمل غيره. وحبلك على غاربك ، يقال : إذا أريد إرسال الإبل بحالة، والحبل – بالفتح بالفارسية رسن والغارب - بكسر الراء المهملة . بند گردن. والحقي بأهلك : أمر من اللحوق. ووهبتك لأهلك : من الهبة. وسرحتك : من التسريح بمعنى الإرسال، والنجاة من المخمصة. وفارقتك : من المفارقة. وأمرك بيدك : أي باختيارك وقصدك ، وهو من كلمات التفويض. وأنت حرة : إذا قال لزوجته يحتمل كونها منفكة عن النكاح كالحرة تنفك عن الرقبة.
[2] His statement: 'And with the rest of them': It is attached to his statement: After reciting the words, one irrevocable divorce occurs. Its apparent meaning is that the words among the kinayah with which revocable divorce occurs are restricted to the three previously mentioned words, and with the rest irrevocable divorce occurs. But it is not so. For it is mentioned in al-Fath and al-Bahr and others that there are other words among the kinayah with which revocable divorce occurs when intended, such as: 'I made the way of your divorce free', 'You are divorced with sukoon on the ta', 'You are more divorced than so-and-so's wife who is divorced', 'You are divorced', 'Divorce is upon you', 'I gifted you your divorce', 'I sold you your divorce', if she says 'I bought it without compensation', 'Take your divorce', 'Allah has willed your divorce', 'Divorce is for you', 'You are divorcée', 'You are not my wife', 'I am not your husband', 'I lent you your divorce', 'I loaned you your divorce', 'May Allah divorce you'. And in some of these words there is disagreement regarding the condition of intention, detailed in Radd al-Muhtar and others. [1] His statement: 'She is bain, binnah, batlah, haram, khaliyyah, barriyyah, habluki 'ala gharibik, ilhaqi bi-ahlik, wahabtuki li-ahlik, sarrahtuki, farqtuki, amruki bi-yadiki, anti hurrah, taqanna'i, takhammari, istitri, aghribi, ukhruji, idhhabi, qumi, ibtaghi al-azwaj': These words all indicate an additional meaning beyond talaq and can bear other meanings. So 'Anti bain' is from baynunah. Al-battah: with fatha on ba and tashdid on ta, meaning cutting and separation. Al-batlah: with fatha on ba on the same pattern, meaning cut and separation. Al-khaliyyah: with fatha on kha, kasr on lam, tashdid on ya, and al-barriyyah: on the same pattern, both from khuluw (emptiness) and bara'ah (freedom) from marriage or other things. The word 'haram' can mean she is forbidden to him or something else. 'Hablu 'ala gharibik' is said when one wants to send away camels in a certain state; 'al-habl' in Persian is 'rasan' and 'al-gharib' with kasr on ra means 'band-i gardan' (neck rope). 'Ilhaqi bi-ahlik' is a command from lahaqa (to join). 'Wahabtuki li-ahlik' is from hibah (gift). 'Sarrahtuki' is from tasrih meaning sending away and deliverance from hardship. 'Faraqtuki' is from mufaraqah (separation). 'Amruki bi-yadiki' means with your choice and intention, and it is a delegation word. 'Anti hurrah' if said to his wife, it can mean she is free from the marriage bond like a free woman is free from slavery.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of kinayah (allusive) words of divorce. The commentator corrects a misunderstanding: it is not only three kinayah phrases that yield revocable (raj'i) divorce. Citing al-Fath and al-Bahr, he lists many phrases that, when intended, result in one revocable divorce. Examples include 'Khallaytu sabila talaqik' and others. Then he turns to phrases that yield irrevocable (bain) divorce, beginning with 'kanat bainun' and similar words. He explains their linguistic meanings: 'bain' comes from baynunah, 'battah' means cutting, 'khaliyyah' means empty of marriage, 'barriyyah' means free, etc. The list includes commands like 'taqanna'i' (veil yourself) and 'idhhabi' (go). These words, being kinayah, require intention to effect talaq; however, the type of talaq (raj'i or bain) is predetermined by the word used when intended. The commentator notes that in some of the revocable phrases there is disagreement about the necessity of intention, a matter detailed in Radd al-Muhtar. The overall ruling is that the classification of these phrases is based on their linguistic connotations and the established practice (ta'ruf) in the school.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An allusive expression that can mean divorce but also something else; it does not effect divorce without intention.
رجعي (raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife without a new marriage during the waiting period.
بائن (bain): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following phrases, when intended, yields a revocable (raj'i) divorce according to the majority of Hanafi sources?
A) 'Anti bain' (أنت بائن)
B) 'Anti khaliyyah' (أنت خلية)
C) 'Khallaytu sabila talaqik' (خلّيت سبيل طلاقك)
D) 'Anti hurrah' (أنت حرة)

Key Takeaway
The classification of kinayah divorce phrases into raj'i and bain depends on the specific word when intended; many phrases yield raj'i beyond the commonly cited three.

Kinayah Words of Divorce: Ruling and Effect of Intention
Ruling on kinayah words with intention scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kinayah words of divorce and apply the rule that intention determines the occurrence and type of talaq.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention; only one ba'in talaq occurs regardless of intended number.

In Hanafi fiqh, divorce can be effected by clear (sarih) words or by kinayah (figurative) words that carry multiple meanings. The key difference is that sarih words do not require intention, while kinayah words require explicit intention for talaq to occur. This lesson unpacks the precise ruling when kinayah words are used, focusing on the effect of the husband's intention on the number and type of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Pinpoint the legal act: a husband pronouncing a kinayah word (e.g., 'akhriji'). The passage states the ruling: if he intends divorce, it falls as one ba'in (irrevocable) talaq, even if he intended two or three.
The ruling is specific to kinayah; sarih words have a different rule.
2
Distinguish by Intention
Analyze the three scenarios: (1) intent to divorce → one ba'in; (2) intent for specific number → still one ba'in; (3) no intent for divorce → passage says 'then three' – interpreted as when circumstantial evidence shows intent to divorce, it becomes three? This ambiguity requires commentary.
The phrase 'fathalath' is debated; some scholars interpret it as three divorces if no specific intention but the context indicates divorce.
3
Define Kinayah and Its Subcategories
The passage defines kinayah as words that can bear other meanings. It provides two categories: words that can be a reply (akhriji, idhhabi, qumi) and words that can be an insult (khaliyyah, bariyyah, battah, haram, ba'in). Understanding the category helps determine the default meaning in context.
The category influences whether the word is likely intended as divorce or not, but intention remains decisive.
4
Apply the Rule to Examples
Take the examples given: if a husband says 'anti khaliyyah' (you are free) intending divorce, one ba'in talaq occurs. If he says it as an insult, no talaq. Practice applying the rule to new examples under Hanafi fiqh.
The rule is consistent: intention is the key.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i Position
The Shafi'i school considers some kinayah words (like 'anti khaliyyah') as sarih (clear) and thus do not require intention. Moreover, they hold that kinayah with intention results in raj'i (revocable) talaq, and the intended number is effective. This highlights a major thamarah al-khilaf.
This comparison is not in the passage but is essential for a full understanding.

Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage from the matn of the text states the ruling for kinayah (figurative) words of divorce in the Hanafi school. The ruling is that if a husband uses a kinayah word such as 'leave' or 'go' and intends divorce, it falls as one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce, even if he intended two or three divorces. This is because the intention of a specific number is not considered effective in kinayah; the default is one ba'in divorce. However, if he did not intend anything else (i.e., he intended the literal meaning or did not intend divorce), then it falls as three divorces? This latter part is interpreted by the commentators as a case where the circumstantial evidence indicates an intention to effect divorce, resulting in three divorces. The passage then defines kinayah: it is a word that can bear other meanings (i.e., not exclusively divorce). Examples of kinayah that can be a reply to a request for divorce are 'akhriji' (leave), 'idhhabi' (go), and 'qumi' (stand). Examples of kinayah that are insults are 'khaliyyah' (free), 'bariyyah' (innocent), 'battah' (cut off), 'haram' (forbidden), and 'ba'in' (separated). The distinction is important for determining the legal consequence based on the husband's intention.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): A word that can bear both the meaning of divorce and other meanings, requiring intention for talaq to occur.
بائنة (ba'in): A divorce that is irrevocable, meaning the husband cannot take his wife back without a new marriage contract and mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No divorce occurs because 'akhriji' is not a clear word of divorce.
B) One ba'in divorce occurs.
C) One raj'i divorce occurs.
D) Three divorces occur.

Key Takeaway
When kinayah words are used with divorce intention, only one ba'in talaq occurs regardless of the intended number; without intention, the ruling depends on context.

Classification of Kinayah Words in Divorce and Their Legal Effect
Classifies kinayah words and links them to contextual rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kinayah words into the three Hanafi categories (رد, سب, neither) and determine when they effect divorce based on intention and contextual indication.

Core Idea
Kinayah words are ambiguous and require intention or clear context to effect divorce; they are classified by whether they can be a response or an insult.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, provides a critical classification of kinayah (indirect) words used in divorce. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rules on talaq, where the husband's intent and context may determine whether a divorce has occurred. The commentary divides kinayah words into three types and explains how different states (anger, satisfaction, discussion of divorce) affect the need for explicit intention.

Guided Path
1
Define kinayah in Hanafi talaq
Understand what makes a word kinayah: it can mean divorce or something else. This sets the stage for the classification.
Kinayah contrasts with sarih (explicit) words like 'anti taliq'.
2
Identify the three categories of kinayah words
Recognize the three groups: (1) words that can be a response (رد) to a divorce request, (2) words that can be an insult (سب), (3) words that can be neither. Note examples for each group.
Examples: اخرجي for رد; خلية for سب; استبرئي for neither.
3
Understand the semantic range of each category
For each category, explore the possible meanings. Words in the 'رد' category can be interpreted as granting or rejecting a request. Words in the 'سب' category convey insult by implying she lacks good qualities. Words in the third category have only non-divorce meanings.
This helps in determining when intention is needed.
4
Apply the requirement of intention or contextual indication
Learn the rule: kinayah words do not effect divorce without intention (niyyah) or a clear circumstantial indication (dalla al-hal). If the context alone indicates divorce, intention is not needed.
This is the core Hanafi principle differing from the Shafi'i school.
5
Distinguish among states of satisfaction, anger, and discussion
In a state of satisfaction, all three categories require intention. In anger, the first two categories require intention; the third category does not effect divorce at all. During discussion of divorce, context often substitutes for intention.
This nuance prevents automatic divorce in emotional contexts.

Yes, they can have other meanings by which divorce is not established, such as 'iddah, and 'istibra' of the womb for companionship, and her uniqueness in beauty and perfection, and her being free (i.e., noble or not a slave), and her choosing herself, and her having the matter in her hand in other than marriage, such as companionship and matters of socializing and explicit statement and separation in companionship and the like. [2] His statement: 'so in satisfaction...' etc.; since the words are of three types, and the situations are three: anger, discussion of divorce, and satisfaction, he wanted to detail their ruling in them. [3] His statement: 'all depend' ; i.e., all words, whether suitable for response or insult or not, because as mentioned, these words can also mean other than divorce, so divorce is not established except by intention or circumstantial indication, and if the latter is not found, then the former is necessary. [4] His statement: 'and in anger' ; i.e., in the state of the husband's anger at his wife, the first two types - i.e., what is suitable for a response and what is suitable for insult - depend on intention; for the circumstantial indication as is appropriate...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, classifies kinayah (metaphorical) words used in divorce. The author first defines kinayah as words that can mean divorce or something else. Then he divides them into three categories: (1) words that can be a response (رد) to a request for divorce or a rejection of that request, like 'ukhruji' (leave), 'idhhabi' (go), 'qumi' (stand up), and similar imperatives implying distance; (2) words that can be an insult (سب), like 'khaliyyah' (empty), 'barriyyah' (free), 'bayyinah' (separated), 'haram' (forbidden), 'ba'in' (severed), which can mean she is empty of good or forbidden from him disrespectfully; (3) words that can neither be a response nor an insult, like 'i'taddi' (observe 'iddah), 'istabri'i rahimak' (purify your womb), 'anti wahidah' (you are one), 'anti hurrah' (you are free), 'ikhtari' (choose), 'amruki biyadik' (your affair is in your hand), 'sarrahtuki' (I released you), 'faraqtuki' (I separated from you). These only have literal meanings unrelated to divorce. The commentary then explains that in a state of satisfaction (رضا), all these words require intention (niyyah) to constitute a divorce, because they are ambiguous. In a state of anger (غضب), the first two categories (response and insult words) also require intention, as the circumstantial indication of anger alone is insufficient. The third category (neither response nor insult) apparently do not effect divorce even in anger because they are not conventionally used for divorce. The underlying principle is that kinayah requires either explicit intention or a clear contextual indication (dalla al-hal) to effect divorce. The text also notes that 'ukhraji' etc. can be used figuratively, so their mere utterance does not automatically mean divorce.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): A word that can mean divorce and something else; it does not directly express divorce but implies it.
ردّ (radd): A response to a request for divorce; the word can be interpreted as either granting the request or rejecting it.
سبّ (sabb): Insult; the word is used to abuse the wife, implying she is devoid of good qualities.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Circumstantial indication that clarifies the speaker's intent, making intention unnecessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only words that can be an insult require intention
D) The first two categories (response and insult) require intention

Key Takeaway
Kinayah words require intention or clear context; their classification helps determine when divorce is effected.

Hanafi Classification of Kināyat al-Ṭalāq and the Role of Intention
Classification of kināyah words and when intention is needed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kināyah words into three categories and apply the Hanafi rule for when intention (niyya) is required for divorce to occur based on the husband's state of mind.

Core Idea
Context can replace intention: in anger and discussion, some kināyah words effect divorce even without explicit niyya.

Divorce by ambiguous words (kināyah) is a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh. Not every word that sounds like divorce actually is – the husband's intention and the surrounding circumstances play a decisive role. In this lesson, we examine a classic passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya that classifies kināyah words into three types and maps them against three mental states: contentment, anger, and discussion of divorce. The goal is to understand when intention is necessary and when contextual indication (dalālat al-ḥāl) suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of kināyah words
List the words mentioned in the passage and sort them into: (1) words that can be interpreted as rejection (radd), (2) words that can be interpreted as insult (sabb), and (3) words that cannot bear either interpretation.
Examples: 'akhrujī' (leave) → rejection; 'khaliyya' (empty) → insult; 'i'taddī' (observe waiting period) → neither.
2
Distinguish the three states of mind
Note that the husband's state is either contentment (riḍā), anger (ghaḍab), or discussion of divorce (mudhākara). These states affect whether contextual indication can replace intention.
Riḍā is defined as absence of anger and not discussing divorce; it is broader than happiness.
3
Apply the rule for each combination
For each of the three states, determine which categories of kināyah require intention and which do not. The rule: in riḍā, all need niyya; in ghaḍab, the first two need niyya; in mudhākara, only the first needs niyya.
The third category (no rejection/insult) never needs intention in anger and discussion, because context clearly indicates divorce.
4
Understand why context can override intention
Reflect on the reasoning: words that are not used for rejection or insult are, in anger or during a divorce discussion, unambiguously about divorce. Thus dalālat al-ḥāl (circumstantial evidence) makes intention unnecessary.
Hanafi fiqh gives significant weight to contextual indication in family law.

And similar: 'Observe the waiting period', and 'Clear your womb', 'You are one', 'You are free', 'Choose', 'Your affair is in your hand', 'I have released you', 'I have separated from you', they cannot bear rejection and insult. So in the state of contentment, all are dependent on intention. In anger, the first two: the intended meaning is 'you are devoid of good or religion' and 'innocent of perfections'. And 'khaliyya' and 'battala': meaning cut off from these things, or from me in lineage, and you are not my equal. And 'bā'in' likewise, from 'baynūn' meaning separation. And 'ḥarām' meaning forbidden, so it is like 'battala', or its meaning is forbidden to be with you due to bad character or nature. These words with these meanings are for insult, and it can be a response to a request for divorce, so the meaning is 'you are empty, innocent, cut off, forbidden from marriage', and these words cannot bear rejection. [1] His statement: 'cannot bear rejection and insult': these words are not used linguistically or customarily for insult or rejection. They can bear other meanings that do not establish divorce, like 'observing the waiting period', 'clearing the womb' for companionship, 'your uniqueness in beauty and perfection', 'being free' meaning noble or non-slave, 'choosing herself', 'her affair being in her hand' in non-marriage matters like companionship and social affairs, 'explicit declaration' and 'separation in companionship' etc. [2] His statement: 'So in contentment...etc': Since the words are of three types and the states are three: anger, discussing divorce, and contentment, he intended to detail their ruling in these states. [3] His statement: 'all are dependent': meaning all words, whether suitable for rejection or insult or not, because these words can bear other than divorce, so divorce is not determined except by intention or context. When the second is absent, the first is necessary. [4] His statement: 'And in anger': in a state of anger of the husband towards the wife, the first two types (suitable for rejection and suitable for insult) are dependent on intention, because the contextual indication also suits rejection and insult, so divorce is not determined except by intention. But the last type does not need intention; divorce occurs with it due to contextual indication. [1] His statement: 'And in': meaning when discussing divorce, like if the wife or someone else asks for her divorce, the first type only is dependent on intention, because rejection is possible in discussion, unlike the other two types, for contextual indication suits only rejection or divorce, not insult or anything else. [2] His statement: 'that it should not be': he indicates that contentment is not the state of activity and joy, because then the states would not be limited to three; rather, it is a broader meaning.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya 'alā Sharḥ al-Wiqāya discusses the legal effect of kināyah (ambiguous) words for divorce depending on the husband's state of mind. The words are divided into three categories: (1) those that can be interpreted as rejection (radd), like 'akhrujī' (leave), 'idhhabī' (go); (2) those that can be interpreted as insult (sabb), like 'khaliyya' (empty), 'bariyya' (innocent), 'ba'ina' (separated), 'harām' (forbidden); and (3) those that cannot bear either interpretation, like 'i'taddī' (observe waiting period), 'istabri'i' (clear womb), 'anti wāḥida' (you are one), 'anti ḥurra' (you are free), 'ikhtārī' (choose), 'amruki bi-yadik' (your affair is in your hand), 'sarrahṭuki' (I released you), 'fāraqtuki' (I separated from you). These words are not used for insult or rejection linguistically or customarily, but can bear other meanings that do not entail divorce. The three states are: riḍā (contentment, where the husband is not angry nor discussing divorce), ghaḍab (anger), and mudhākarat al-ṭalāq (discussing divorce). In contentment, all three categories require intention (niyya) for divorce to occur. In anger, the first two categories (suitable for rejection and insult) require intention; the third category (no rejection/insult) does not require intention—divorce occurs regardless, because the context of anger indicates a desire to divorce. In discussing divorce, the first category (suitable for rejection) requires intention; the other two do not require intention. The reasoning is that the contextual indication (qarīnat al-ḥāl) in these situations can clarify the intended meaning. The passage emphasizes that the state of 'riḍā' does not mean a state of happiness but simply the absence of anger or discussion of divorce; it is a broader category. This nuanced classification is a distinctive feature of Hanafi fiqh in dealing with kināyat al-ṭalāq.

Key Terms
كناية الطلاق (kināyat al-ṭalāq): Ambiguous expressions that can imply divorce but are not explicit; the effect depends on intention and context.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual indication from the circumstances (e.g., anger, discussion) that clarifies the intended meaning of a kināyah word.
نية (niyya): Intention of the husband to effect divorce when using a kināyah word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the state of anger (ghaḍab), which category of kināyah words does NOT require intention (niyya) for divorce to occur?
A) Words suitable for rejection (e.g., 'ukhrujī')
B) Words suitable for insult (e.g., 'khaliyya')
C) Words that cannot bear rejection or insult (e.g., 'i'taddī')
D) All categories require intention in anger

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the need for intention in kināyat al-ṭalāq depends on the word category and the husband's state of mind: context can substitute for intention when the circumstance clearly points to divorce.

Talaq — Divorce

Intention in Kinayah Divorce Based on State
Ruling on kinayah divorce dependence on intention in three states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on intention for each type of kinayah divorce expression in the three states (rida, ghadab, mudhakarah) and EXPLAIN why the ruling varies based on dalalat al-hal.

Core Idea
Context determines whether an indirect divorce expression requires intention — the stronger the contextual clue, the less need for explicit niyyah.

In Hanafi law, not all divorce expressions are explicit. Some are kinayah (indirect) and their effect depends on the speaker’s intention. However, the context or state of the speaker can make the meaning so clear that intention becomes unnecessary. This lesson unpacks that principle across three states: normal (rida), anger (ghadab), and discussion of divorce (mudhakarah). You will learn which expressions require intention and which do not, based on the rational proof of dalalat al-hal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of kinayah expressions
The text categorizes kinayah into: (1) what is suitable as a reply (ma yasluhu raddan), (2) what is suitable as abuse (ma yasluhu sabban), and (3) what is not suitable as either (ma la yasluhu raddan wa la sabban). Understand these categories as they are the basis for the ruling.
Examples: 'Observe your 'idda' is a reply; 'You are free' is abuse; 'Go away' is pure.
2
Understand the three states of the speaker
The states are: rida (normal, not angry nor discussing divorce), ghadab (anger), and mudhakarat al-talaq (discussing divorce). Rida is defined as the absence of the other two states.
The state is determined at the time of utterance.
3
Apply the rule: in rida, all types require intention
In a normal state, no context strongly indicates divorce, so all three kinayah types require niyyah to effect divorce.
This is the baseline ruling.
4
Apply the rule: in ghadab, the first two require intention, the third does not
In anger, the first two types (reply and abuse) could still be interpreted differently, so intention is needed. The third type (pure abuse) is so closely associated with divorce in anger that context alone suffices.
The third type effects divorce even without intention.
5
Apply the rule: in mudhakarah, only the first requires intention, the others do not
When divorce is being discussed, only expressions that could be a reply (first type) might be ambiguous; the others are clearly about divorce due to the context.
The second and third types effect divorce without intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي مذاكرة الطلاق الأول فقط. والمراد بحالة الرضا: أن لا يكون حالة غضب، ولا مذاكرة الطلاق، فحينئذ يتوقف الأقسام الثلاثة على النية. وفي حال الغضب يتوقف الأولان: أي ما يصلح رداً وما يصلح سباً على النية، إن نوى الطلاق يقع به الطلاق، وإن لم ينو لا يقع، وأما القسم الأخير: وهو ما لا يصلح رداً ولا سباً يقع به الطلاق، وإن لم ينو. وفي حال مذاكرة الطلاق يتوقف الأول: أي ما يصلح رداً على النية، أما الآخران، وهما ما يصلح سباً وما لا يحتمل الرد والسب، فيقع بهما الطلاق وإن لم ينو. الطلاق يناسب الرد والسبب أيضاً فلا يتعين الطلاق إلا بالنية، وأما النوع الأخير فلا يحتاج إلى النية، بل يقع الطلاق بها لدلالة الحال. قوله: وفي؛ يعني عند مذاكرة الطلاق، كأن يسأل الزوجة أو غيرها طلاقها، يتوقف النوع الأول فقط على النية؛ لكون الرد محتملاً عند المذاكرة، بخلاف النوعين الآخرين، فإن دلالة الحال لا يناسب إلا الرد أو الطلاق لا السب ولا غيره. قوله: أن لا يكون؛ يشير به إلى أنه ليس المراد بها حالة النشاط والسرور، فإنه حينئذ لا تنحصر الحالة في الثلاثة، بل المراد بها ما هو أعم منها. (انتهى من النقاية ص 87). جدول توضيحي للمسألة: جواب فقط سب وجواب رد وجواب اعتدي، استبرئي خلية، برية أخرجي، اذهبي تلزم النية تلزم النية تلزم النية رضا يقع بلا نية تلزم النية تلزم النية غضب يقع بلا نية يقع بلا نية تلزم النية مذاكرة
And in discussing divorce, only the first [type] is suspended [on intention]. And what is meant by the state of satisfaction is that it is not a state of anger nor a state of discussing divorce; so then all three types are suspended on intention. In the state of anger, the first two—i.e., what is suitable as a reply and what is suitable as abuse—are suspended on intention: if he intends divorce, divorce occurs by them, and if he does not intend, it does not occur. As for the last type—that which is not suitable as a reply nor as abuse—divorce occurs by it even if he does not intend. In the state of discussing divorce, the first type—i.e., what is suitable as a reply—is suspended on intention; as for the other two, which are what is suitable as abuse and what does not bear reply or abuse, divorce occurs by them even if he does not intend. Divorce is also appropriate for reply and cause, so divorce is not determined except by intention. As for the last type, it does not need intention; rather, divorce occurs by it due to contextual evidence. He said: 'And in'—meaning, in the case of discussing divorce, such as when the wife or another asks for her divorce, only the first type is suspended on intention because reply is possible in discussion, unlike the other two types, for contextual evidence does not indicate except reply or divorce, not abuse or anything else. He said: 'That it is not'—he indicates by it that what is meant is not the state of activity and joy, for then the state is not confined to the three; rather, what is meant is more general than them. (End from al-Nuqayah p. 87). Explanatory table of the issue: Response only, abuse and response, abuse, 'Observe your 'idda, seek Tuhrah', 'Khaliah, Bariyah', 'Leave, go'; in satisfaction: intention required, intention required, intention required; in anger: occurs without intention, intention required, intention required; in discussion: occurs without intention, occurs without intention, intention required.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the Hanafi ruling on kinayah (indirect) divorce expressions and how they depend on intention (niyyah) in three different states: satisfaction (rida), anger (ghadab), and discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq). The passage first states the general principle: in a normal state of satisfaction, all three types of kinayah require intention. In anger, the first two types (those suitable as a reply or as abuse) require intention, but the third type (pure abuse) does not; divorce occurs regardless of intention due to the strong implication of the context. In the context of discussing divorce, only the first type (suitable as a reply) requires intention; the other two types (suitable as abuse and pure abuse) do not require intention. The commentator explains that the phrase 'dallalat al-hal' (contextual evidence) is the proof: in anger and discussion, the context makes divorce the clear meaning for certain expressions. A table is provided to summarize the rulings for the three types of expressions (reply, abuse, pure abuse) across the three states. The authority of this ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, and it is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
كناية الطلاق (kināyat al-ṭalāq): Divorce expressions that are indirect and can be interpreted as divorce or something else, requiring intention to effect divorce.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual evidence that indicates the intended meaning; in this context, it determines whether divorce is clearly implied without need for intention.
نية (niyyah): Intention; in kinayah divorce, it is required to specify that divorce is meant unless the context makes it clear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All three types
B) Only the type that is pure abuse
C) The first two types: suitable as reply and suitable as abuse
D) Only the type suitable as reply

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the context (dalalat al-hal) to determine when intention is needed for kinayah divorce, resulting in different rulings for the three states.

Definition of Tafwid and Its Distinction from Wakalah
Distinguishing tafwid from other forms of delegated divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tafwid (tamlik) and distinguish it from wakalah (agency) based on the criterion of who acts by whose opinion.

Core Idea
Tamlik empowers the wife to act by her own opinion; wakalah binds the agent to the principal's opinion.

This passage introduces the chapter on tafwid by first listing three ways divorce can be executed by someone other than the husband: by messenger (risalah), by agent (wakalah), and by delegation (tafwid). The text then clarifies the crucial difference between tafwid (also called tamlik) and wakalah – a distinction rooted in whose opinion governs the action. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly applying the rulings of delegated divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of divorce by another
The passage states that after covering divorce by the husband, the text addresses divorce executed by another. List them: risalah (message), wakalah (agency), tafwid (delegation).
2
Focus on the definition of tafwid
Tafwid is defined as tamlik – giving the wife ownership of the divorce. Contrast this with wakalah where the husband appoints an agent.
3
Analyze the distinction between tamlik and wakalah
The key difference: the one given ownership (mamlik) acts by his/her own opinion (ra'y), while the agent (wakil) acts by the principal's opinion. The passage further refines 'opinion' vs 'will' (mashiyyah).
This distinction determines the wife's autonomy in tafwid.
4
Apply the distinction to the three expressions of tafwid
Recognize that tafwid itself has three sub-forms (takhyeer, amr bil-yad, mashiyyah) which will be detailed later.
These are mentioned in al-Hidayah.

ولمن قيل لها: طلقي نفسك. باب التفويض. [1] قوله: باب التفويض؛ لما ذكر الطلاق الذي يوقعه الزوج بنفسه بنوعيه: الصريح والكناية أراد أن يذكر ما يوقعه غيره وأنواعه ثلاثة: 1. رسالة؛ كأن يقول لرجل: اذهب إلى فلانة وقل لها: إن زوجك يقول لك: أنت طالق أو اختاري. 2. ووكالة؛ كأن يوكل رجلاً بتطليق الزوجة. 3. وتفويض؛ وهو تمليك الزوجة الطلاق. والفرق بين التمليك والتوكيل: أن المملك له يعمل برأي نفسه، والوكيل برأي موكله، أو يقال: الوكيل يعمل بمشيئة غيره، والمالك يعمل بمشيئة نفسه. (1) والفرق بين الرأي والمشيئة على ما في «الفتح»: أن العمل بالرأي عمل بما يراه أصوب بلا اعتبار كونه لنفسه أو لغيره، والعمل بالمشيئة عمل باختياره ابتداء بلا اعتبار مطابقة أمر الآمر، ولا اعتبار معنى الأصوبية. [2] قوله: ولمن؛ المراد به الزوجة بقرينة قوله لها، وأشار بذكر الألفاظ الثلاثة إلى أن للتفويض صوراً ثلاثة ذكر صاحب «الهداية» كلاً منها في فصل: التخيير نحو: اختاري، والأمر باليد نحو: أمرك بيدك، والمشيئة نحو: طلقي نفسك، وقد يقسم التفويض إلى صريح وكناية. فالصريح: هو ما اشتمل على لفظ الطلاق أو ما ينوب منابه ولا يحتمل غيره، نحو: طلقي؛ ولذا يقع به واحد رجعي، ولا يحتاج التفويض إلى نية، وتصح فيه نية الثلاث على ما سيأتي.
And for her who is told: 'Divorce yourself.' Chapter of Delegation. [1] He said: 'Chapter of Delegation'—when he mentioned the divorce that the husband executes himself in its two types: explicit and indirect, he intended to mention what someone else executes. Its types are three: 1. Message: such as saying to a man, 'Go to so-and-so and tell her: Your husband says to you: You are divorced or choose.' 2. Agency: such as appointing a man to divorce the wife. 3. Delegation: which is making the wife possess divorce. The difference between making possess and appointing as agent: that the one who is made to possess acts by his own opinion, and the agent acts by the opinion of his principal; or it is said: the agent acts by the will of another, and the owner acts by his own will. (1) And the difference between opinion and will according to what is in 'al-Fath': action by opinion is action by what one sees as more correct without consideration of whether it is for oneself or another, and action by will is action by one's choice initially without consideration of conformity to the command of the commander, nor consideration of the meaning of correctness. [2] He said: 'And for her'—what is meant is the wife by the indication of his saying 'her,' and he indicated by mentioning the three expressions that delegation has three forms mentioned by the author of 'al-Hidayah' each in a chapter: election such as 'choose,' command by hand such as 'your affair is in your hand,' and will such as 'divorce yourself.' And delegation is divided into explicit and indirect. The explicit: is what includes the word of divorce or what takes its place and does not bear other than it, such as 'divorce me'; therefore, by it falls one revocable [divorce], and delegation does not need intention, and intention of three is valid in it as will come.

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on tafwid (delegation) of divorce in the commentary on al-Wiqayah. It explains that after discussing divorce initiated by the husband directly (both explicit and indirect), the text now turns to divorce initiated by someone other than the husband. There are three types: (1) through a messenger (risalah), (2) through agency (wakalah), and (3) through tafwid, which is giving the wife ownership (tamlik) of the divorce. The key difference between tamlik and wakalah is that the person given ownership acts based on his/her own opinion (ra'y), while the agent acts based on the principal's opinion. The distinction is further refined: opinion is acting by what one deems most correct, while will (mashiyyah) is acting purely by choice regardless of correctness or alignment with the command. The passage then identifies three forms of tafwid corresponding to the expressions used: takhyeer (choice), amr bil-yad (command by hand), and mashiyyah (will). Finally, tafwid is divided into explicit (sarih) and indirect (kinayah). Explicit tafwid uses the word 'talaq' or its equivalent and does not require intention; it results in one revocable divorce, but intention for three divorces is valid as will be explained later.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of divorce; giving the wife ownership of divorce so she can divorce herself.
تمليك (tamlīk): Making the wife possess the right of divorce; a synonym for tafwid in this context.
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; appointing someone to execute the divorce on behalf of the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental difference between tafwid (tamlik) and wakalah?
A) In tafwid, the wife acts by the husband's opinion; in wakalah, the agent acts by his own opinion.
B) In tafwid, the wife acts by her own opinion; in wakalah, the agent acts by the principal's opinion.
C) Both allow the wife to act independently.
D) Tafwid is invalid, wakalah is valid.

Key Takeaway
Tafwid (tamlik) grants the wife ownership of the divorce to act by her own opinion, distinctly from wakalah where the agent acts by the principal's opinion.

Types and Rulings of Tafwid: Explicit and Indirect
Classifies tafwid and states rulings for explicit tafwid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main categories of tafwid (sarih and kinayah) and state the ruling regarding intention and the number of divorces in explicit tafwid.

Core Idea
Explicit tafwid using the word 'talaq' results in one revocable divorce without need for intention, but the wife's intention of three is valid.

The passage continues to break down tafwid into two main categories: explicit (sarih) and indirect (kinayah). Explicit tafwid uses unambiguous words like 'talaq' and has specific rulings: it does not require intention (niyyah) because the meaning is clear, and it results in one revocable divorce. However, if the wife intends three divorces, her intention is effective. This lesson examines these rulings and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of tafwid
The passage divides tafwid into sarih (explicit) and kinayah (indirect). Explicit tafwid includes the word 'talaq' or its unambiguous equivalent.
2
Determine the ruling for explicit tafwid
The passage states that explicit tafwid results in one revocable divorce (raj'i). No intention (niyyah) is needed for this effect.
3
Note the exception for intention of three
Even though no intention is required for one divorce, if the wife intends three divorces, her intention is valid and three divorces occur.
This will be detailed later.
4
Connect to the broader classification
Recognize that tafwid also has three expression-based forms (takhyeer, amr bil-yad, mashiyyah) which may be sarih or kinayah depending on wording.

And for her who is told: 'Divorce yourself.' Chapter of Delegation. [1] He said: 'Chapter of Delegation'—when he mentioned the divorce that the husband executes himself in its two types: explicit and indirect, he intended to mention what someone else executes. Tafwid (Delegation of Divorce): Definition, Distinction, and Conditions
Defines tafwid, distinguishes from agency, explains sarih/kinayah, majlis condition with ijma proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tafwid, distinguish it from tawkil, classify explicit vs. implicit tafwid, and apply the condition that the wife must exercise her divorce option in the same session.

Core Idea
In tafwid, the wife owns the divorce and acts by her own will; her option expires when her session ends unless timed.

After studying divorce initiated by the husband, we now turn to divorce delegated to others. This lesson focuses on tafwid, where the husband empowers his wife to divorce herself. We will clarify the definition, distinguish it from agency, and explore the critical condition that ties the wife's option to the session in which she learns of the delegation. Understanding these details is essential to correctly apply this mechanism in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three forms of divorce by another
The text lists risalah (message), wakalah (agency), and tafwid (delegation). Recognize that tafwid specifically means making the wife owner of the divorce.
Tafwid is also called tamlik in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Distinguish tafwid from tawkil
In tafwid, the wife acts by her own opinion and will; in tawkil, the agent acts by the principal's opinion and will. The difference between 'ra'y' and 'mashiah' is explained from Fath al-Qadir.
Tamlik gives the wife autonomy; tawkil imposes the husband's directives.
3
Classify tafwid into sarih and kinayah
Sarih uses the word 'divorce' or its unambiguous equivalent (e.g., 'Tallaqi nafsaki') and requires no intention. Kinayah uses phrases like 'Ikhtari' or 'Amruki bi-yadik' and requires intention of divorce.
Sarih yields one revocable divorce; kinayah yields divorce only with intention.
4
Analyze the majlis condition and the ijma proof
The wife must exercise her option in the same session she learns of the delegation. The session is her own, not the husband's. The text states 'upon this, the consensus of the Companions is established,' making this rule definitive.
If the session changes (e.g., she stands up), her option is lost.
5
Consider time-limited delegation and session change
If the husband sets a time limit (e.g., 'today'), the option expires after that time. Changing the session can be real (moving to another place) or constructive (an action breaking continuity).
The commentary distinguishes between real and constructive changes of session.

And for the one to whom it is said: 'Divorce yourself.' Chapter of Tafwid. Section on Choice. [1] His statement: Chapter of Tafwid; when he mentioned the divorce that the husband himself initiates, in its two types: explicit and implicit, he intended to mention what someone else initiates, and its types are three: 1. Message: as if he says to a man: go to so-and-so and tell her: your husband says to you: you are divorced, or choose. 2. Agency: as if he appoints a man to divorce the wife. 3. Tafwid: which is making the wife the owner of divorce. And the difference between tamlik (making owner) and tawkil (appointing agent): that the one given ownership acts according to his own opinion, and the agent acts according to the opinion of his principal; or it is said: the agent acts according to the will of another, and the owner acts according to his own will. (1) And the difference between opinion and will, according to what is in al-Fath: that acting by opinion is acting according to what he sees as most correct, without consideration of whether it is for himself or another; and acting by will is acting by his choice from the beginning, without consideration of conformity to the command of the commander, nor consideration of the meaning of most-correctness. [2] His statement: 'and for the one'; what is meant is the wife, by the context of his statement 'to her'. [0] And the implicit: that which admits this and something else, like 'choose' and 'your affair is in your hand', for it admits the possibility of choice in another matter, so it needs intention. [1] His statement: 'or your affair is in your hand'; its meaning: your affair concerning separation is in your grasp and choice, so do as you wish. [2] His statement: 'with the intention of divorce'; a restriction for the last two, for they need the intention of divorce, unlike the first because it is explicit. So if he did not intend in the last two, it is not tafwid, neither judicially nor religiously, in a state of contentment. But in a state of anger or discussion, he is not believed judicially that he did not intend divorce. (1) As in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'she knew of it' is a clause describing the session, meaning that she may divorce herself in the session in which she knew of his delegation, whether face-to-face and address, or by news and writing. And in it is an indication that the consideration is her session, not the session of his delegation. So if he gave her choice and then stood up, it is not invalid. So if her session changes, her option is nullified. Upon this, the consensus of the Companions is established. [4] His statement: 'even if long'; the waw is conjunctive; meaning even if that session in which she knew of it is long, a day or more. And it is conditioned that the delegation not be time-limited by the husband; otherwise, her option is nullified after the passing of that time, whether she knew of it or not. So if he said: 'I have made it for her that she may divorce herself today', her option becomes null after the day passes, even if she knew of it after that. As in al-Fath. [0] His statement: 'as long as she has not stood up'; meaning as long as the one given choice has not risen from that session or done an act that cuts the session. And he indicated by it that changing the session is of two types: real change: which is by moving to another place. (1) al-Bahr al-Raiq (3:336).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the chapter of Tafwid (delegation of divorce) from al-Hidayah. The author first explains the broader context: after discussing divorce initiated by the husband himself (explicit and implicit), the text now turns to divorce initiated by someone else, which has three types: message (risalah), agency (wakalah), and tafwid (empowerment). Tafwid is defined as making the wife the owner of the divorce (tamlik al-zawjah al-talaq). The key distinction between tamlik and tawkil is that the owner (the wife in tafwid) acts according to her own opinion and will, while the agent acts according to the principal's opinion and will. The author then elaborates on the difference between 'opinion' (ra'y) and 'will' (mashiah) from Fath al-Qadir: opinion is acting based on what one deems most correct regardless of self-interest, while will is acting by one's own choice without necessarily conforming to the commander's wish.

The text then discusses the forms of tafwid: 'Choose', 'Your affair is in your hand', and 'Divorce yourself'. Tafwid is divided into explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinaya). Explicit tafwid uses the word 'divorce' or its unambiguous equivalent, like 'Divorce yourself'. This type does not require intention, and one revocable divorce occurs. The implicit forms ('choose', 'your affair is in your hand') admit other possibilities, so intention of divorce is necessary; without it, it is not valid tafwid.

A crucial condition is that the wife must exercise her divorce option in the same session (majlis) in which she learned of the delegation, even if that session is long (extending over a day or more). The session is considered her session, not the husband's. If the husband gives her choice and then stands up, her option is not invalidated. However, if she changes her session (e.g., leaves the place or engages in an act that breaks the session), her option expires. This ruling is established by the consensus of the Companions (ijma al-sahabah). The text also notes that if the husband sets a time limit for the delegation (e.g., 'today'), the option expires after that time whether she knew of it or not.

The author uses the phrase 'as long as she has not stood up' to indicate that standing up from the session constitutes a real change of session, which terminates the option. Other actions that break the session are also considered.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Making the wife the owner of the divorce (tamlik al-zawjah al-talaq), i.e., delegating the power to divorce to her.
تمليك (tamlik): Literally 'making owner'; in tafwid, the wife is made the owner of the divorce, free to exercise at her discretion.
توكيل (tawkil): Appointing an agent to perform the divorce on behalf of the husband; the agent must follow the husband's instructions.
مجلس (majlis): The session in which the wife learns of the delegation; she must exercise her option in that same session without it being broken.
صريح (sarih): Explicit tafwid using the word 'divorce' or its unambiguous equivalent; does not require intention.
كناية (kinayah): Implicit tafwid using phrases like 'Choose' or 'Your affair is in your hand' that could also refer to other matters; requires intention of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what distinguishes sarih (explicit) from kinayah (implicit) in tafwid (delegation of divorce)?
A) Sarih requires intention, while kinayah does not.
B) Sarih does not require intention, while kinayah does.
C) Sarih results in three divorces, while kinayah results in one.
D) Sarih is only valid if the wife is present, while kinayah is valid by message.

Key Takeaway
Tafwid empowers the wife to divorce herself, but she must do so in the same session she learns of the delegation; explicit terms require no intention, while implicit terms do.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Majlis Condition in Delegation of Divorce (Tafwid)
Commentary on tafwid majlis condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of the majlis in tafwid, distinguish between real and constructive change of session, and identify the Hanafi proof (ijma and qiyas) for this ruling.

Core Idea
The wife's option to divorce herself in tafwid lapses if she leaves the session or does an act indicating aversion – the majlis condition is essential.

This lesson examines the crucial ruling that a wife who has been delegated the right to divorce herself (tafwid) must exercise that right in the same session (majlis) in which she learns of the delegation. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the nature of the session, the distinction between real and constructive change, and the proof for this condition. Understanding this ruling is vital for correctly applying delegated divorce in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that tafwid is a grant (tamlik) of the right to divorce by the husband to the wife. The ruling: the wife may divorce herself only in the same session she learns of the delegation.
This is not agency (wakalah); it is a transfer of ownership of the right.
2
Understand the session condition
The session is defined as the continuous period during which the wife remains in the same place without a break. The consideration is her session, not the husband's – if the husband stands up, the option remains until she changes her session.
The text uses 'her session' – majlis see 'alimt bihi' (the session she knows of it).
3
Distinguish real and constructive change
Real change occurs when she physically moves to another place. Constructive change occurs when she begins an act that indicates turning away, such as speaking about unrelated matters. Mere standing up without leaving is constructive change.
Examples: putting on a garment or drinking do not break the session, but extraneous speech does.
4
Examine the proof
The ruling is established by consensus of the Companions (ijma al-sahabah) and analogy to sale (tamlik). Just as acceptance in sale must be in the same session, so must acceptance of the grant of divorce.
The text states 'upon this the consensus of the Companions is established'.
5
Apply to scenarios
Test your understanding with scenarios: if the husband time-limits the option, the session condition is overridden by the time limit; if the wife leaves the room, the option lapses; if she stands up only, it also lapses as constructive change.
Time-limited delegation expires after the specified time even if she remains in session.

Book of Divorce: Chapter on Delegation and the Command Hand and Option. 'Or your command is in your hand, or choose with the intention of divorce: she divorces herself in a session she knows of it, even if long, as long as she has not stood up.' 'Or your command is in your hand, or choose, with intention of divorce: she divorces herself in a session she knows of it, even if long.' His statement: 'she divorces herself' is the subject, and 'so she may do as she wishes' is its predicate. Then he explained the session with his statement: 'as long as she has not stood up.' Kinayah is that which admits another meaning, like 'choose' and 'your command is in your hand', for it admits interpretation of choice in another matter, so it requires intention. [1] His statement: 'or your command' means your command in the matter of separation is in your grasp and choice, so do what you wish. [2] His statement: 'with intention of divorce' is a condition for the latter two, for they require intention of divorce unlike the first because it is explicit. So if he did not intend in the latter two, it is not a delegation, neither judicially nor religiously in a state of contentment. As for a state of anger or discussion, he is not believed judicially that he did not intend divorce. Thus in al-Bahr. [3] His statement: 'she knows of it' is a clause describing 'session', meaning she may divorce herself in the session in which she learned of his delegation, verbally, by address, or by information, or by writing. It indicates that the consideration is her session, not his session of delegation. So if he gave her the option then stood up, it does not nullify. If her session changes, her option lapses. Upon this the consensus of the Companions is established. [4] His statement: 'even if long' the waw is conjunctive; meaning even if that session in which she learned is long, a day or more. But it is conditional that the delegation not be time-limited by the husband, otherwise her option lapses after that time passes, whether she knew of it or not. So if he said: 'I made it so that she can divorce herself today,' her option lapses after the day passes, even if she learned after it. Thus in al-Fath. [0] His statement: 'as long as she has not stood up' means as long as the woman given the option has not stood up from that session or done an act that cuts the session. He indicated by this that the change of session is of two types: Real change: which is by moving to another place. Constructive change: which is by commencing an act that indicates turning away. He guarded by this condition against what is not like that, for if he gave her the option and she put on a garment or drank, her option does not lapse. Included in 'act' is extraneous speech, not speech related to the option. Thus in al-Bahr. The meaning of 'standing up' in the text is either moving, as it is said 'he stood up from his session' meaning he moved to another place, and then it refers to real change, or standing up from sitting, if she was sitting and stood on her feet, and then both cases are constructive change because by mere standing the session does not change; rather the option lapses because it is a sign of turning away, as indicated by the author of al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'so it does not belong after it' etc.; the reason for that is that delegation is a grant (tamlik), so it depends on acceptance in the session, and it is not an agency (wakalah), therefore he cannot revoke it. [2] His statement: 'from the session' apparently where he transposed 'standing' with 'from', indicates that the intended meaning of 'standing' in the text is going to another session, by consensus of the Companions, and because it is a grant of the act from her, and grants require a response in the session, like in sale; for the moments of the session are considered as one hour, except that the session is sometimes changed by leaving it, and sometimes by engaging in another act; for the session of eating is different from the session of debating, and the session of fighting is different from both. Her option lapses by mere standing because it is a sign of turning away. [3] His statement: 'and witnesses' in the genitive, coordinate with 'father', and the following sentence is descriptive.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from the chapter on delegation (tafwid) in divorce. The author explains the conditions under which a wife who has been given the option to divorce herself may exercise that right. The key ruling is that she must divorce herself in the same session (majlis) in which she learned of the delegation, and she loses the option if she leaves that session or does an act that indicates she is turning away from the choice. The text distinguishes between explicit words of divorce (sarih) which do not require intention, and kinayah such as 'choose' or 'your command is in your hand' which require the intention of divorce. The session is defined as the period during which she remains in the same place without a change that breaks the continuity. The change of session is of two types: real (physical departure to another place) and constructive (commencing an act that shows disregard, like extraneous speech). Importantly, the consideration is the wife's session, not the husband's; if the husband stands up after delegating, the option remains as long as she stays. The rationale provided is that delegation is a type of tamlik (grant), which requires acceptance in the session by analogy with sale (bay'), and therefore it is not revocable like agency (wakalah). The author also notes the consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahabah) on the majlis condition, giving it strong authority.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the right to divorce herself.
مجلس (majlis): The session or gathering in which the wife learns of the delegation and in which she must exercise her option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of change ends the wife's option in tafwid?
A) Real change only (leaving the place).
B) Constructive change only (starting an act of aversion).
C) Both real and constructive change.
D) Only standing up from a sitting position.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, delegation of divorce (tafwid) is a grant (tamlik) that must be accepted in the same session; the wife loses the option if the session changes, based on the consensus of the Companions.

Actions That Do Not Terminate the Woman's Option in Delegated Divorce
Explanation of actions preserving the option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify actions that do not terminate a woman's option in delegated divorce and explain the distinction between actual and legal change of session based on Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Only disinterest (i'rad) or leaving the session ends the option; trivial actions like dressing or drinking do not.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband delegates divorce to his wife (tafwid), she has a limited option to accept or decline during the same session. However, not every action after the offer terminates her choice. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that actions such as sitting, leaning, or consulting her father preserve the option because they do not indicate disinterest. Understanding these distinctions is key to correctly applying the ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Recognize that the wife's option in tafwid remains valid as long as she does not act in a way that shows disinterest or actually leaves the session. The passage lists actions that do not break the option.
The option is a grant (tamlik) that requires acceptance in the same session, similar to sale.
2
Distinguish actual vs. legal change of session
Actual change occurs when she physically leaves the place. Legal change occurs when she performs an action clearly indicating disinterest, even if she stays in the same location.
Standing up from a sitting position is considered legal change because it signals disinterest, even if she does not leave.
3
Analyze the enumerated actions
Examine each action mentioned: sitting of a standing woman, leaning of a sitting woman, sitting after leaning, the father calling for consultation, and witnesses witnessing. These actions show the woman is still considering or seeking advice, not rejecting the offer.
These actions do not constitute i'rad (disinterest).
4
Apply the principle to trivial actions
Understand that trivial actions like putting on a garment, drinking, or extraneous speech do not nullify the option because they do not demonstrate disinterest.
Extraneous speech here means speech unrelated to the divorce choice.
5
Review the qiyas (analogy) to sale
The Hanafi school analogizes tafwid to a sale contract: both are grants (tamlik) that require acceptance in the same session. The session is considered a single unit; leaving or showing disinterest terminates the option in both cases.
This qiyas is the legal proof (dalil) supporting the ruling.

[٢] قوله: عن المجلس؛ ظاهره حيث عدّى القيام بعن، يشير إلى أن المراد بالقيام في «المتن» هو الذهاب إلى مجلس بإجماع الصحابة لله، ولأنه تمليك الفعل منها، والتمليكات تقتضي جواباً في المجلس، كما في البيع؛ لأنّ ساعات المجلس اعتبرت ساعة واحدة، إلا أن المجلس تارة يتبدل بالذهاب عنه، ومرة بالاشتغال بعمل آخر؛ إذ مجلس الأكل غير مجلس المناظرة، ومجلس القتال غيرهما، ويبطل خيارها بمجرد القيام؛ لأنه دليل الإعراض. Or [if she] does not do what cuts it [the option] after it, and the sitting of the standing woman, and the leaning of the sitting woman, and the sitting of the leaning woman, and the father's calling for consultation, and the witnesses' witnessing their pronouncement. 'Or she does not do what cuts it after it' means that she does not have the choice after her rising from the council, nor after an act that cuts it, for the council is changed by one of two matters: either by rising, or by an act that is not of the same type as what preceded. And the sitting of the standing woman, and the leaning of the sitting woman, and the sitting of the leaning woman, and the father's calling for consultation, and the witnesses' witnessing their pronouncement. And a legal change: that is by undertaking an act that indicates disinterest, and he guarded by this restriction against what is not like that, for if he gave her the choice and she put on a garment or drank, her option is not nullified, and included in 'act' is extraneous speech, not speech related to the choice. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. And what is meant by 'rising' in the text is either moving, as is said: he rose from his council, i.e., moved to another place, and then it is an indication of actual change; or rising from sitting, meaning she was given choice while sitting and she stood on her feet, and then both cases are from the legal change, because by mere rising the council does not change, but the option is nullified by it because it is evidence of disinterest, as indicated by the author of 'al-Hidayah'. [1] His statement: 'i.e., it is not... etc.'; the aspect of that is that delegation is a grant (tamlik) so it depends on acceptance in the council, and it is not an agency (tawkil), hence he cannot revoke it. [2] His statement: 'from the council'; apparently, where he modified 'rising' with 'from', it indicates that what is meant by 'rising' in the text is going to a council by consensus of the Companions, and because it is a grant of the act from her, and grants require a response in the council, as in sale; for the hours of the council are considered as one hour, except that the council is sometimes changed by leaving it, and sometimes by engaging in another work; for the council of eating is not the council of debating, and the council of fighting is different from both, and her option is nullified by mere rising because it is evidence of disinterest. [3] His statement: 'and the witnesses' with genitive case; conjoined to 'the father', and the following sentence is an adjective. (1) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (3:337-338).
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn from Al-Wiqayah. The author explains which specific actions do not terminate a woman's option (khiyar) in a delegated divorce (tafwid). The key principle is that the option is tied to the session (majlis) of offer. The session can be terminated either actually (by leaving the place) or legally (by undertaking an action that indicates disinterest, i'rad). The matn enumerates several actions—sitting by the standing woman, leaning by the sitting woman, sitting after leaning, and the father's calling for consultation, and the witnesses witnessing their own pronouncement. These actions do not indicate disinterest; rather, they show the woman is still considering or seeking advice. The commentary further clarifies that trivial actions like putting on a garment or drinking do not invalidate the option. The crucial distinction is between actions that demonstrate acceptance versus those showing rejection. The author also discusses the meaning of 'rising' in the matn: it can mean actual relocation (actually change of session) or simply standing up (which is legally considered a change of session because it indicates disinterest). The commentary concludes with an analogy to sale, where the option in a sale contract is also contingent upon the session. The Hanafi school relies on this reasoning (istihsan and qiyas) to determine the validity of the option.

Key Terms
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife giving her the choice to divorce herself or not.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session or meeting place where the offer of delegation is made.
الإعراض (al-i'rad): Disinterest or turning away from accepting the option.
التبدل الحقيقي (al-tabadul al-haqiqi): Actual change of the council by physically leaving the place.
التبدل الحكمي (al-tabadul al-hukmi): Legal change of the council by an action indicating disinterest, even if the person remains in the same place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions does NOT terminate a woman's option in a delegated divorce?
A) The woman stands up and walks to another room to fetch a book.
B) The woman puts on a garment after the offer.
C) The woman remains seated but begins to speak about something unrelated to the divorce.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the wife's option in delegated divorce remains valid unless she actually leaves the session or performs an action clearly indicating disinterest; neutral or consultative acts do not terminate it.

Rulings on Mounts, Ships, and Intention of Triple Divorce in Delegated Choice
Expansion and reasoning on conditions of option integrity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actions that nullify a woman's option in delegated divorce and those that do not, and state the ruling on intending multiple divorces when saying 'I choose myself.'

Core Idea
The option in delegated divorce is lost by actions showing disinterest; walking a mount shows disinterest, but ship movement does not, and 'I choose' cannot effect multiple divorces.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies nuanced scenarios in delegated divorce (talaq bi al-ikhtiyar). It explains that a woman's option remains valid when her mount stops or when she is on a ship because those actions do not indicate disinterest, unlike the animal's walking. Additionally, if she says 'I choose myself' intending three divorces, only one divorce takes effect because the word 'choose' is indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its effect on the option
Determine whether the action in question (stopping an animal, ship movement, saying 'I choose' with intention) nullifies the woman's option or leaves it intact.
The passage examines three distinct scenarios: mount cessation, ship, and intention.
2
Distinguish attributed vs. non-attributed actions
Classify actions as either attributed to the woman (like walking) or not attributed (like ship motion). Attributed actions that indicate disinterest nullify the option; unattributed ones do not.
Stopping is attributed but indicates interest; walking is attributed and indicates disinterest.
3
Apply the principle of indication
For each action, assess whether it is a 'dalil al-i'rad' (indication of disinterest) or not. Only actions that clearly show she has turned away from the option cut it.
Sitting down, leaning, or stopping do not indicate disinterest; walking, however, does.
4
Analyze the ship analogy
Understand why a ship is like a house: the rider has no control over its movement, so moving from side to side or the ship's motion is not attributed to her and does not nullify the option.
Contrast with a riding animal where the woman can control its speed and direction.
5
Derive the rule for intention of multiples
Apply the principle that 'ikhtiyar' (choice) is indivisible: the word 'I choose' refers to a single action, so intending three divorces does not increase the number.
This is from Al-Hidayah: 'al-ikhtiyar la yatanawwa'.'
